From time to time amid the toilsome scholarship and labored eloquence, someone is bound to ask, as Irenaeus did long ago in his arguments with the gnostics, “Why not forget all this play of abstraction, allegory, mysticism, and metaphor and simply take the words of the Bible literally as they stand?”1 At once comes the knee-jerk reply of a dozen deep thinkers, “Who is going to tell us exactly what the words do mean; since when have the doctors agreed on them?” Today as ever, the test cases for literalism are, as you might know, the creation story and the resurrection story. We are told much more about the resurrection than about the creation; the whole of creation mind you, of heaven and earth, angels, men, animals, planets, elements, galaxies, the works, is all accounted for in a page and a half, all “instantaneously and simultaneously.” Yet strangely, the ministers who gulped down that camel with ease, gag and strangle on the resurrection. Since ancient times the doctors of the church have been loathe to accept it literally, and they still are.

The Gospel of Matthew

I have only time to discuss one or two issues today, and I will confine myself to only one Gospel, that of Matthew, since that is as far as I have got in my Gospel Doctrine class. Let us make a quick survey leading up to today’s subjects for discussion—the Beatitudes (upon which books are still pouring forth) and the Lord’s Prayer.

At the outset of Matthew we are introduced to a number of miraculous happenings; an article appearing very recently by a learned divine finds the virgin birth totally unacceptable. The star of Bethlehem on the other hand, since it may have been a real phenomenon, is repeatedly rediscovered, most recently as a conjunction of Jupiter and Saturn.

In chapter 3 we read that John the Baptist had a large following and that he denounced the Pharisees and Sadducees, just as Jesus was to do later, for failing to bring forth fruits. He baptized Jesus (with the testimony of the Father—“This is my beloved Son”), who then took over the work. John the Baptist had come as Elijah, the perennial predecessor of the Messiah.

Next we have the Lord’s personal confrontation with Satan (chap. 4), who as ever wants to give the orders. A remarkable feature of Matthew is that almost every episode of the story follows immediately upon the one preceding it, connected with a temporal adjective: “when,” “at that time,” “while.” The whole story is an unbroken chain of connected and necessary incidents. Thus immediately after Satan had sought out and tried to enlist the Lord as a follower, the Lord himself begins enlisting those who are to become the pillars of the Church—Peter, Andrew, James, and John (Matthew 4:18–22); all were to remain true and faithful, his intimate confidants and the leaders of his Church. Immediately thereafter we find Jesus preaching and healing “throughout all Syria” with vast multitudes following him (Matthew 4:24–25), a phenomenon not unfamiliar in Jewish history. But unlike other leaders of the people, Jesus does not exploit his advantage when he has the crowd passionately pursuing him. He leaves them and goes off usually to a mountain by himself, where his disciples come to join him, where he discusses with them intimate things, “it is given unto you to know the mysteries of the kingdom of heaven, but to them it is not given” (Matthew 13:11). He is giving them instructions for their work in building up the Church.

The Beatitudes

The first twelve verses of chapter 5 are the Beatitudes. They answer the question Who is qualified to be admitted to the Church?—for the “kingdom of heaven” is, as we shall see, the way the Church is regularly designated hereafter. Those who are qualified for membership are “the poor in spirit: for theirs is the kingdom of heaven”—that is, they rate the kingdom. Likewise qualified are those that mourn and need comfort, and the meek who shall inherit the earth (that is, hereafter when the kingdom comes to its own). Those who hunger and thirst after righteousness, who are eager to accept the message and be accepted—who else could be better qualified? Likewise deserving are the merciful who shall be paid in kind, the pure in heart, they alone whose confidence waxes strong in the presence of God (having no hidden vices to rankle the subconscious with guilt), those who are persecuted for being righteous, and finally those who are thoroughly unpopular in our present society; what they can hope for is “your reward in heaven” (Matthew 5:12). Notice that these are all low-profile people, the same that Isaiah recommends for blessings, having the exact opposite of all the qualities that make for success in our present world.

In your King James Bible verse 13 is rightly marked as beginning a new section; the Lord is now addressing the disciples as the leaders of the Church. He addresses them as the salt of the earth, of which only a little is needed but is of prime significance; if they cannot function properly everything falls flat. It is the disciples, not the milling crowds that are the salt of the earth—one only needs a little, but it makes all the difference. Then in a new section, which is also marked beginning with verse 17, he reminds them that there is only one law and one gospel. He contrasts them with the leaders of the Jews, the scribes and Pharisees (v. 20). Then he gives them strict rules of behavior and emphasizes their high responsibility.

The Lord’s Prayer

In chapter 6 he tells his disciples how they must differ from other leaders and be examples to the people, and then in a miracle of condensation he sets forth the purpose and rationale of the Church. It is all in the Lord’s Prayer:

Our Father (Matthew 6:9). We know that he is Jesus’ Father—but ours as well? Yes, but the world will not accept it; even now the pious authors go into all sorts of contortions to get around it. But it was the same in Jesus’ own day. Jesus was put to death for the specific claim that God was his Father, that he was the literal Son of God. As we read later on, “Tell us whether thou be the Christ, the Son of God. Jesus saith unto him, Thou hast said: . . . Then the high priest rent his clothes, saying, He hath spoken blasphemy; . . . What think ye? They answered and said, He is guilty of death” (Matthew 26:63–66).

God as a Father is rarely mentioned in the Old Testament: at times, however, in the New Testament the concept is everywhere: My Father, Your Father, the Father in Heaven, etc. What is wrong? Either Christ is teaching a drastically new doctrine—and that is what the scribes and Pharisees accused him of, or else the Jews have strayed from an older gospel and lost it. Which was it? The prophets leave us in no doubt. Their constant refrain is that Israel has strayed from the law and broken the everlasting covenant. The opening words in Isaiah are those of an offended Father: “I have nourished and brought up children, and they have rebelled against me. The ox knoweth his owner, and the ass his master’s crib: but Israel doth not know, my people doth not consider” (Isaiah 1:2–3). And the last prophet, Malachi, writes: “A son honoureth his father, and a servant his master: if then I be a father, where is mine honour? and if I be a master, where is my fear? . . . O priests, that despise my name. And ye say, Wherein have we despised thy name?” (Malachi 1:6).

Which art in heaven (Matthew 6:9). Is heaven a place? Of course scholars all say that this is just a figure of speech, but whole chapters of John (14–17) labor to make it as explicit as possible that we are all in the same family with God as our Father. A few years ago the journals displayed a number of studies on the locus, place, or situation in the Bible—that is, the coming and going of angels, and especially of the Lord: “Where I go, ye cannot come . . . for I go to the Father . . . to prepare a place for you . . . they watched until he was out of sight . . . in this same manner shall he return,” etc. (cf. John 8:22; 14:12; 14:2; Acts 1:11).

The problem of place and the best possible introduction to its meaning in the Lord’s Prayer is Solomon’s prayer at the dedication of the temple (see 1 Kings 8:13–49):

21. . . . I have set there a place for the ark, wherein is the covenant. . . . 22. [Then he] stood before the altar . . . and spread forth his hands toward heaven: . . .

27. . . . Will God indeed dwell on the earth? behold, the heaven and heaven of heavens cannot contain thee; how much less this house that I have builded? 28. Yet have thou respect unto the prayer of thy servant, . . . 29. That thine eyes may be open toward this house night and day, even toward the place of which thou hast said, My name shall be there: that thou mayest hearken unto the prayer which thy servant shall make toward this place. 30. And hearken thou to the supplication of thy servant, and of thy people Israel, when they shall pray toward this place: and hear thou in heaven thy dwelling place. ...

31. If any man trespass . . . and an oath be laid upon him, . . . and the oath come before thine altar. . . . 32. Then hear thou in heaven, and do, and judge thy servants.

33. When thy people . . . have sinned against thee, and shall turn again to thee, and confess thy name, and pray, and make supplication unto thee in this house: 34. Then

hear thou in heaven, and forgive the sin of thy people. . . .

35. When the heaven is shut up, and there is no rain, . . . If they pray toward this place, and confess thy name, and turn from their sin. . . . 36. Then hear thou in heaven, and forgive the sin of thy servants.

37. If there be in the land famine, . . . pestilence, . . . locust, . . . if their enemy besiege, . . . whatsoever plague, whatsoever sickness . . . 38. What prayer and supplication . . . be made by any man, . . . which shall know every man [every man involved] the plague of his own heart, and spread forth his [own] hands [individually or collectively] toward this house: 39. Then hear thou in heaven thy dwelling place, and forgive. . . .

41. Moreover concerning a stranger, that is not of thy people Israel, but cometh out of a far country for thy name’s sake; 42. (For they shall hear of thy great name . . . ) when he shall come and pray toward this house; 43. Hear thou in heaven thy dwelling place, . . . that all people of the earth may know thy name, to fear thee, . . . that they may know that this house, which I have builded, is called by thy name.

44. If thy people go out to battle . . . whithersoever thou shalt send them, and shall pray . . . toward the city which thou hast chosen, and toward the house that I have built for thy name: 45. Then hear thou in heaven their prayer . . . and maintain their cause. 46. If they sin, . . . 47. [are] carried captives, and repent, . . . 48. [then they] pray unto thee toward their land, which thou gavest unto their fathers, the city which thou hast chosen, and the house which I have built for thy name: 49. Then hear thou their prayer . . . in heaven thy dwelling place.

The situation is clear: God does not live in the temple; neither do his people. They do not go there to see him, and he does not come down to meet them there, but when they wish to make serious contact with him they face the temple and concentrate their thoughts in that direction. Instead of his person they deal with him through that all-important name. Now consider how the Lord here instructs the disciples: “After this manner therefore pray ye: Our Father which art in heaven” (Matthew 6:9). God is not described here as being everywhere, as his influence is, nor described as dwelling in our hearts. This is a personal relationship between child and parent; he is expressly referred to as “our Father in heaven.”

The temple is the common place of contact which God has appointed for his people to come to make a covenant with him, and, if they are afar off, to focus their thoughts. There must be a central place. It is only a building, but men are only men, and God knows what he is doing.

Hallowed be thy name (Matthew 6:9). Hallowed means respected, acknowledged, reverenced, its authority held in awe. In Solomon’s prayer as in the Lord’s Prayer, the name, like the building, is something to focus on. In a person’s absence we must have his name to identify him to others and keep him in mind ourselves. If you have never seen the king face to face or do not remember having met him and can’t be certain exactly what image you should hold of him, then the name is all important. You do not know exactly how others think of him or what their relationship to him is, but the one thing you have in common with them and must always hold to is the name.

Thy kingdom come (Matthew 6:10). A kingdom is a region in which a king’s will is not only law but is reverenced, respected, and obeyed, as God’s will has rarely been on this earth. When God’s kingdom comes, and not until then will thy will be done, not occasionally by fits and starts, and here or there as it is now, but as the sole guiding principle of all action, right here in earth, not as it is done now, but totally and completely as it is in heaven. The community in which God’s will is acknowledged and obeyed is the Church, and there is no doubt that that is what is meant by the kingdom of heaven, as is clearly explained in the next chapter. The kingdom is the Church as it should be.

Give us this day our daily bread (Matthew 6:11). This next verse looks like a sudden break, a shift from the celestial and eternal order to very mundane and practical things. In a famous novel Joseph Conrad describes a ship sinking in a terrible storm—a sailor is clinging to the stays on the mast and shouting the Lord’s Prayer at the top of his voice. The author, who is describing his own experience, says he found it a singularly odd time and place to be worrying about daily bread. But this apparent shift of ground is absolutely vital, since this is a prayer of people living in the world. “Thy kingdom come” is an optative, a wish, it has not yet happened; things are not on earth as they are in heaven where God’s will is everyone’s will. What is preventing it, what is holding up this consummation so devoutly to be wished? It is that other plan by which men are in bondage to each other, dependent on each other, in debt to each other—an obstacle to the kingdom which the law of Moses removes in the only way it can be removed, by the direct bounty of God and “the Lord’s release” in which all human debts were canceled.

It is the need for daily bread that enables men to exploit each other and deny free agency. If God does not provide daily bread, most people will have to sell their lives for bread. He does not want that; he wants us to have it as a gift. A gift does not have to be earned—other things must be earned, and they present a much harder challenge, but not the elements for preserving life. Air and water are more vital even than food, and some are trying to make even them marketable, controlling and holding back supply until their price is met. Eternal salvation must be earned, but the chance to take the test is given to all freely; you do not buy a gift from a giver or engage in a free-for-all to see who can grab the most of those gifts which a generous Father has provided for us all. The prime source of food in Israel, as everywhere else, is the rain, and the Lord tells us in Matthew that he lets his rain fall equally on the just and the unjust alike, the deserving and the undeserving. His direct gift of food to Israel was manna. The manna was literally daily bread, replenished fresh each day, but lasting for just one day only— after 24 hours it rotted and stank and could not be hoarded; it was not negotiable, no futures, no sales. Is there a subtle implication in the fact that when used for business it would be a foul and dirty thing? (I seem to recall lines about filthy lucre, the deceitfulness of riches, thy money perish with thee, etc.) And that went on for forty years? Was God trying to ruin their characters by giving them all a free lunch? No, they had plenty of tests to undergo, but they were not tests in acquiring stuff or negotiating deals, as we see in what immediately follows.

And forgive us our debts, as we forgive our debtors (Matthew 6:12). Some translators have obligingly tried to soften the passage by turning debt and debtors into the generic “trespasses,” but what is here used is strictly business language referring to money owed. God recognized that Israel could not be free without the Lord’s release. Which meant that all debts were canceled every seven years. There was simply no other way; otherwise, men inevitably lose freedom of action, bound by debts both owed and owing. We ask God to forgive us what we owe him in the exact proportion as we forget the debts that are owed to us. This theme is repeated in Matthew in some powerful parables. And what do we owe God? Everything, says King Benjamin. Though a man should work zealously in the kingdom all day long everyday, he would still remain an “unprofitable servant”—that is, consuming more than he produces. We are all in God’s debt, says Benjamin, and forever shall be, which means that our only hope is to show him how much we want to be delivered by following his instructions and canceling all debts to us. The law of the release was the only way to get out of the debtors’ prison in which men keep each other. It is God’s will that men cancel debts to each other, and until they cease holding debts, accounts, mortgages, bonds, etc., over each other, God’s kingdom will never be established on earth as it is in heaven.

But why would God allow such a state of things to persist since plainly he is against it? It is a problem that baffled the doctors of the Church and remained unsolved—the problem of evil: God is either weak because he cannot prevent such injustice or vicious because he permits it. O misera necessitas, cries St. Augustine, non posse non peccare—that is, the most terrible thing that can happen to us is that we are able to sin.2 And so we come to the paradoxical statement which cannot be avoided:

Lead us not into temptation (Matthew 6:13). The fact that we are being constantly tempted means that God permits it. Here the prayer is “do not let us go too far,” eis peirasmon— that is, on the way of being tested. Would God lead us into temptation? Why not, if we are here on earth to be tempted? He will allow Satan, our common enemy, to try man and to tempt him. This recognizes that being tested is part of God’s plan. He can let the test go as far as he wants to, as in the case of Abraham and Isaac, a subject of shock and abhorrence by the critics. Like Abraham, we are being tested as part of God’s plan; to know no temptation implies a static condition of life—but they shall be as the angels. Irenaeus, replying to the gnostics, says that the chance to take a test here on earth makes us “envied of the angels.”3 We may ask God to temper the test as he did with Cain after he complained, “My punishment is greater than I can bear” (Moses 5:38).

But deliver us from evil (Matthew 6:13). The word here for “deliver” means snatch, rescue, drag—the implication being that the test is no trivial one. St. Augustine does not see why God simply does not allow temptation by forbidding evil to exist. The common answer of conventional Christianity to the paradox of evil in a world created by God is that there is no evil really; it is only an illusion. Evil is evil precisely because it is not—it is purely negation, etc. But Joseph Smith in the grove learned better. To say “I am no devil” is Satan’s most effective ploy.

So Satan is allowed to tempt us but not to destroy us—as with Job, the order is “but spare his life.” The word is rhysai, to save in time, to snatch out of danger, and it shows that one is to be exposed to danger; there is no plea to forbid evil to exist or to prevent it from coming near us, but only not to let things go too far, to save us in time. Satan leads us into temptation, and if we yield to temptation, then we depend on a Savior to clear the way back to God’s presence after we have learned good and evil by experience.

For thine is the kingdom, and the power, and the glory, for ever. Amen (Matthew 6:13). The instructions to Israel from the time of the exodus to the Dead Sea Scrolls is that when a prayer is offered all who hear it must cry Amen to show that it is their prayer too. Some have said that the doxology was added later to the Lord’s Prayer and is therefore an anachronism in the Book of Mormon. Joachim Jeremias has shown from ancient examples, Jewish and Christian, that in the oldest version of the Lord’s Prayer the doxology was bound to be included; the prayer would not be complete without it.4 It was naturally assumed that this would be added to any prayer. It is the eschatological statement: the delivering from evil is immediately followed by a return to the heavenly sphere in which the prayer began: “We will provide a Savior, deliverer, rescuer that they may again return to our presence,” which is indeed the kingdom and the power and the glory which is to last forever—the eternal life to come. The Lord’s Prayer follows a pattern familiar from ancient ceremonies and “mysteries” which follow a threefold order: (1) the archē, which is protology, the prologue in heaven, the preexistence; (2) the omphalos, the present center which is history, the part that concerns our present condition, the scene of the contest in which the issue is still in doubt, and (3) the sphragis or seal which is placed upon the final accomplishment; it is the consummation and ends in heaven. Without 1 and 3 you cannot understand number 2—the daily bread, debts and debtors, the evil situation in which they have placed us. The omphalos of the prayer is the cry de profundis, out of the depths, which all the Saints have uttered at one time or another. We need only think of Enoch, Abraham, Moses, Elijah, and the Lord himself.

The rest of the chapter is of great importance in explaining and expanding on the prayer. God is not just a model or example to follow in forgiving: only as far as we forgive others will he forgive us. If we don’t forgive them, he will not forgive us (Matthew 6:14–15). We show our true feelings toward God in the way we treat people. Later on he emphasizes the principle, unto “the least of these” (Matthew 25:40). The less important the person you help, the greater will be your blessing, for in helping such there is no ulterior motive or gain envisaged. It is interesting that the verse asking to be delivered from evil is immediately followed by a verse telling how we go about qualifying for such deliverance, “If ye forgive men their trespasses, your Heavenly Father will also forgive you.” Next, we are told not to be skythropos (dour, self-righteous). Make no effort to appear righteous; lean over backward not to, for your good deeds are a secret between you and God (Matthew 6:16–18). And we are told to avoid coveting, or brosis, which is any kind of decay or consumption, oxidation, the fate of all material things. In the next verse the treasures in heaven definitely refer to the preexistence (v. 20). You have treasures up there without realizing it. They are waiting for you. According to a rich apocryphal literature, they are being kept on hold from your former existence, and one of the great tragedies of sin in this world is that you lose so much after having already won it. The theme continues. You cannot mingle the two worlds: “where your treasure is, there will your heart be also” (v. 21); you cannot “serve two masters” (v. 24); the Lord really means it when he says “take no thought for your life, what ye shall eat, or what ye shall drink” (v. 25), etc. He knows that this is a radical doctrine that has upset the disciples, but he continues to force it on them: Food? He takes care of the birds. Clothes? Consider the lilies of the field. The trouble is, he tells them, they don’t have any faith, they should give it a try (see v. 32). The people of the world everywhere see the economy as the big problem, but as the Lord often told Israel, Christ now tells the disciples, “You are not to be like them.”

The Lord’s Prayer is more than just a way of getting through life, a code of morals, or a pattern of behavior. It is an appeal to a Father we have known before and hope to dwell with hereafter. It asks for help in carrying out the first and greatest commandment. In this very short prayer, God, man as the child of God, and fellowman are all put in their proper relationship, which is the closest possible family association, approaching identity. The tempter and his methods are introduced, without which the statement of the gospel plan would be incomplete, for the prayer by its very nature is an appeal from those in distress who are supplicating for something much better than what they have. What we want is to dwell in the Father’s kingdom under the sole dominion of his divine will by his power and in his glory forever and ever. Jesus proceeds to explain the relationship to God of those to whom he imparts the prayer: The Father will deal with you exactly as you deal with other humans; he will even withhold his kindness from you if you withhold it from them. But as far as credit for what you do, you are making no deals with men; you are not out to impress them or make points with them (a theme strongly emphasized in the Gospel of Matthew). Any consideration of reward is wholly between you and your Heavenly Father; and any credit you are able to get from man, seeking it deliberately by various means, you will lose with God.

Notes

^1. Irenaeus, Contra Haereses (Against the Heresies) 1, proem.

^2. Augustine, On Correction and Grace 33.

^3. Irenaeus, Against the Heresies 4.37.3–6, in PG 7:1101–3.

^4. Joachim Jeremias, “The Lord’s Prayer in Modern Research,” Christian News from Israel 14 (April 1963): 10.

24

Turning the Time Over to . . . Priesthood

Originally a Sunday School lesson, this was published in Sunstone, December 1990, 10–11. A few minor changes have been made, including adding a footnote and correcting some of the citations.

[Sunstone] Editor’s Note: Several weeks ago, a friend gave us a copy of this piece, purportedly written by Hugh Nibley. When we read it, it had the feel of Nibley’s classic social commentaries but not the look; for one thing, Nibley doesn’t use multiple exclamation points. Obviously, ours was an uncorrected transcription from a clandestine recording. After a second person gave us a copy that was given to him by his BYU religion teacher, we contacted Brother Nibley. He corrected our copy and allowed us to publish an “authorized version” of what was originally part of a Sunday School lesson.

The priesthood ceases to be effective when exercised “in any degree of unrighteousness” (D&C 121:37), but it operates by the spirit, and the spirit is not deceived but is exquisitely sensitive to the slightest color of fraud, pretension, self-will, ambition, cruelty, etc. “When we undertake . . . to exercise control or dominion or compulsion upon the souls of the children of men, in any degree of unrighteousness, behold, the heavens withdraw themselves; the Spirit of the Lord is grieved; and when it is withdrawn, Amen to the priesthood or the authority of that man” (D&C 121:37). But what about the righteous dominion of the priesthood? That can be easily recognized, for it operates “only by persuasion, by long-suffering, by gentleness and meekness, and by love unfeigned; By kindness and pure knowledge, which shall greatly enlarge the soul without hypocrisy, and without guile,” with “bowels . . . full of charity towards all men” (121:41–42, 45). Even in the eternities the power of the priesthood flows “without compulsory means . . . forever and ever” (121:46).

Who can deny such a power to another? No man. Who can bestow it on another? No man. We like to think that the Church is divided into those who have it and those who don’t have it; but it is the purest folly to assume that we can tell who has it and who does not. God alone knows who is righteous and how righteous; yet “the rights of the priesthood are inseparably connected with the powers of heaven,” and those “cannot be controlled nor handled only upon the principles of righteousness” (D&C 121:36). The result is, that if there is anyone who really holds the priesthood, no one is in a position to say who it is—only by the power to command the spirits and the elements is such a gift apparent. But as far as commanding or directing other people, there every man must decide for himself.

One valuable hint the Lord has given us, however, namely the assurance that of all those who “hold” the priesthood almost none really possess it. “That the rights of the priesthood . . . may be conferred upon us, it is true,” making us formally priesthood holders, “but when we undertake . . . to exercise control or dominion or compulsion upon the souls of the children of men, in any degree of unrighteousness” the priesthood is void (D&C 121:36–37). And this is how it is in “almost all” cases in the Church: “We have learned by sad experience that it is the nature and disposition of almost all men, as soon as they get a little authority, as they suppose, they will immediately begin to exercise unrighteous dominion. Hence many are called, but few are chosen” (D&C 121:39–40).

What does one have to do to be chosen?

First, one may not set one’s heart “upon the things of this world” (D&C 121:35)—so much for the priesthood as something to show off; then, one may not aspire to the honors of men—so much for the priesthood as something for prestige. One cannot exercise any power of the priesthood in any degree of unrighteousness—this in full recognition of the fact that “it is the nature . . . of almost all men” to do that very thing as soon as they think they have power and authority.

This leaves a few humble, unpretentious, and unworldly people as the sole holders of a valid priesthood. It is the few “humble followers of Christ” who are the strength of the Church throughout much of the Book of Mormon history (see 2 Nephi 28:14; Helaman 6:39).

What irony. As far as the whole world is concerned, the priesthood is a thing of value which is cruel to withhold from anyone, because it enhances one’s status and dignity among his fellows, whether inside the Church or outside. And yet the one thing that renders that priesthood completely null and void is to treat it as something to aspire to among one’s fellows. Priesthood is strictly an arrangement between the individual priesthood holder and his brethren in the eternal worlds, as personal and private as anything can be.

We might as well recognize the fact that whatever we say and do in righteousness is going to be misinterpreted. The only way we can make things easier for ourselves in the world is to go the way of the world. It would be hard to deny that the peace and prosperity of the Church in the past years has been largely the fruit of willingness to go the way the world goes.

Where all truth is encompassed in one great whole, to raise one question is to raise many others, and any issue relevant to the gospel inevitably leads to a discussion of the whole thing.

Is not the priesthood everything? Not on this earth. On this earth it is nothing, and as soon as we try to use it for any kind of status, power, rule, or authority, it automatically cancels out.

To repeat, as we are prone to do for lack of wit, for those who hold the priesthood on this earth, it is, Brigham Young said, an “onerous” burden, not a prize. One cannot give orders to another by the priesthood. One cannot use it to acquire prestige, fame, or wealth. Far from impressing one’s fellow men, it is held in derision by them. The moment one tries to make honor or glory, or exercise dominion by the priesthood, “amen to the priesthood . . . of that man”—it automatically becomes null and void. What good is it then? Over whom does it exercise dominion? Over the spirits and over the elements—but not over one’s fellow men, who cannot under any circumstances be deprived of their complete free agency.

Though some may find it hard to believe, I find no cause for boasting in my priesthood—nothing is easier than conferring it upon one, but that is only the beginning; for it to be a real power requires a degree of concentration, dedication, and self-discipline which few ever attain to, and for the rest priesthood is not a blessing but a terrible risk. The priesthood is not a badge of office to be worn as a feather in a cap.

Do we really believe the first vision? Thousands of Latter-day Saints attest to it every Fast Sunday; but when the earliest, fullest, and best account of the first vision, dictated by the Prophet at the age of 26 to Frederick G. Williams, was discovered and published in 1968 it caused not the slightest ripple of interest in the Church. It is enough, apparently, to know that God has spoken again from the heavens—never mind what he said.

The most useful lesson is the silence of heaven on this particular issue in the light of our own woeful ignorance. There is a connection between the two. Where the people do not seek for wisdom and knowledge, God will not give it to them, and so they remain in ignorance, and may not ask for help from above.

Nothing pleases God more than to have his children “seek greater light and knowledge”—it was for that that Adam, Abraham, Enoch, Moses, and Joseph Smith were rewarded with the richest blessings. Nothing displeases him more than to have them “seek for power, and authority, and riches” (3 Nephi 6:15). Through the years the Latter-day Saints have consistently sought not for the former but for the latter. It is only right and proper that we should stew in our own juice for a while.

“I sought for the blessings of the fathers, . . . desiring also to be one who possessed great knowledge, and to be a greater follower of righteousness, and to possess a greater knowledge” (Abraham 1:2).

Twice he repeats it—he wants knowledge. Up to the last, even after he had learned all the doctrines of salvation, Adam still “seeks for greater light and knowledge” and for such knowledge we should seek eternally. But what do we hear? A former president of the BYU pompously announced at a convention of educators that we at the BYU are not seeking for truth, because we have the truth! This is where we stand today. It is common at the BYU for students to protest against being taught anything they did not know before: “Wherefore murmur ye, because that ye shall receive more of my word? . . . And because that I have spoken one word ye need not suppose that I cannot speak another; for my work is not yet finished; neither shall it be until the end of man” (2 Nephi 29:8–9).

The fact is that the Latter-day Saints “will not search knowledge, nor understand great knowledge, when it is given unto them in plainness, even as plain as word can be” (2 Nephi 32:7). They simply are just not interested. How little we know about things. How little we want to know. The information is there, far more abundant than we have been willing to realize, if we will only reach out for it. To wait for a revelation on the subject is foolish until we have exhausted all the resources already placed at our disposal.

The strong prejudice has long been extended to the Indians by many Mormons in high position, yet the Mormons alone of all the people in the world believe the Indians to be the true blood of Israel, no less.

Such attitudes are strengthened by the snobbery of American suburbia; the Mormons like to think of themselves as WASPS—yet it was the rural, white, Christian, Protestant Americans upon whom the Lord with his own lips excoriated to the youthful prophet; they are all hypocrites, said he: “They were all wrong . . . all their creeds were an abomination in his sight . . . those professors were all corrupt; that: ‘they draw near to me with their lips, but their hearts are far from me . . . having a form of godliness, but they deny the power thereof’” (Joseph Smith—History 1:19).

There are those in the Church who would identify Zion with “Executive Meadows, the Exclusive Condominium for the Right People.”

Repeat of repeat: Over whom does it exercise power, then? Over the spirits and over the elements—never over one’s fellow men, whose free agency is absolute and inalienable.

Christ commanded the spirits, and they obeyed him; he commanded the elements and they obeyed him, but men he would not command, and he rebuked the apostles at Capernaum for suggesting it. “How often would I have gathered you together . . . but ye would not!” (D&C 43:24).

What, then is the priesthood on this earth? It is what Brigham Young and the Twelve wrote in the Times and Seasons in 1839; they called the priesthood an “onerous duty,” a load to be borne.1 Very few men on earth, including those in the Church are really qualified. In terms of prestige, status, power, influence, pleasure, privilege, “power, and authority, and riches” (3 Nephi 6:15), the priesthood has absolutely nothing to offer. The world laughs at it, the Latter-day Saints abuse or ignore it, those who take it seriously do so in “fear and trembling.”

Note

^1. Manuscript History of Brigham Young, 4 July 1839; “To the Elders of the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints,” Times and Seasons 1/1 (1939): 13.

25

Tribute to Krešimir Ćosić

This was written on the occasion of Krešimir Ćosić’s death on 25 May 1995.

My first acquaintance with Krešimir Ćosić1 was when he came to my office with a question. As a brilliant and precocious basketball star, whose name—as our missionaries discovered—was almost a household word in some parts of Europe, he had been welcomed into the charmed circle of the jet set of the time and knew the delights of fashionable high living. But in considering his true condition, he said, he was greatly distressed—it all seemed so perfectly empty, of course. But what else was there? If that was the sort of thing that everybody wanted, why do we exist at all? Naturally, I quoted the scriptures to him: “Men are, that they might have joy” (2 Nephi 2:25). He did not know the word joy—his English was just beginning. “Radost,” I said, trying the Russian word. He responded with a smile of immense satisfaction—Joy was his element, not the dreary and laborious pleasures of sophisticated celebrities, but the spontaneous childlike happiness of sharing an eager awareness of each other. “This is my work and my glory,” said God, to share “immortality and eternal life” with absolutely everyone (Moses 1:39).

Shortly after, Krešimir asked me to do some work for him in the temple. To me, almost a stranger, his style of gentle persuasion seemed to come on a bit strong, and I was very sick at the time—I could not possibly go to the temple. But I went, and at the very first words of the initiatory work, I suddenly felt a giant’s strength and a great surge of joy. After the session I insisted on walking the two miles home alone in high spirits. Who was this man for whose work Krešimir was so irresistibly insistent? When I got to the house Krešimir was waiting and told me the story. It was a relative who had lived in a previous century, a powerful noble in the land and a leader among the people. It was a time of strife, hatred, and internecine power struggle among factions and communities that had long been wasting the land. What could ever check or halt the obsessive evil that endlessly fed on itself? The great man thought of an ingenious plan. In different parts of the country he built manors or hostels for each of his various sons (I think there were seven). In each of these a sort of open house was to be practiced, not only toward the general public, but for the sons to visit and enjoy each other’s company from time to time. According to Krešimir, this cultivating of the natural tendency of God’s children to like each other did indeed have an uplifting and pacifying effect in all the land.

This was my first association with Krešimir when he was as much of an enigma to me as to others. But I never again treated his urgings and suggestions lightly; there was a purpose to everything he did.

When Krešimir first came to the Brigham Young University, he mingled with a student element which was anything but prudish; nobody knew better than he the hankypanky that went on in the outer reaches of Well-to-do. One day he came to me and said, “There are a hundred reasons why I should not join the Church, and only one reason why I should—because it is true.” So he asked me to baptize him. He did not want to make a public relations gimmick of a sacred ordinance and had a very effective way of keeping it from spreading abroad: when asked if he had really been baptized, he would give you his usual merry smile and ask with a twinkle, “What do you think?” The things of this world he did not take too seriously. After losing a championship game to Colorado by the narrowest of margins, he came to the house, and Phyllis commiserated with him— “You must have felt very bad about it.” “Yes,” he said, “I did feel very bad about it—for twenty minutes!”

That was the source of his confidence and joy, an unfailing sense of values. He saw things in a different perspective from the rest of the world, and this explains his unshakable cheerfulness and sense of fun—he wanted to share the joke with you, the joke being that there is far more to existence than being a world-famous athlete. He was extraordinary enough to give one the feeling of being almost with an alien. And, indeed, newspaper writers and others found it most strange that a much-sought-after athletic talent should end up at a school most people had never heard of. His unpredictable career led him to situations of singular influence where no striving ambition could have taken him.

There is a natural ambiguity about administering to the sick—we don’t know everything, and what we want and ask for is not necessarily for the best; even faith to move mountains implies an equivalent amount of knowledge, which we do not have. So naturally we pray for what we want—what else? We are invited to express our wants and know where we stand. But who would be foolish enough to insist that the final decision be his own? Krešimir Ćosić has always been a step beyond or at least beside the normal run of men. His life was ever promising new trials and triumphs, but on a plane not common to mortals; this gives some of us the assurance that all is not over by any means.

His friend,

H. Nibley

Note

^1. Krešimir Ćosić died of cancer on 25 May 1995 at the age of 46 in Washington, DC. He was a popular basketball star at Brigham Young University in the early 1970s. He returned to Yugoslavia to be a player and coach of the national basketball team. He served as Croatia’s deputy ambassador and acting ambassador to the United States of America.

26

Graveside Service Address for Joel Erik Myres

In a week I shall have my ninety-first birthday. What do I have to look forward to after that? The answer—as much as I ever had. Are we going anywhere in this life? The other side is where the real work is being done: “There was a space granted unto man in which he might repent; therefore this life became a probationary state; a time to prepare to meet God; a time to prepare for that endless state which has been spoken of by us, which is after the resurrection of the dead” (Alma 12:24).

The history of the race, like the life of the individual, has been a succession of tests. They come in dispensations. Joseph Smith in one book has given us comprehensive accounts of seven great dispensations of the gospel. It was an exercise unmatched in “extreme scholarship,” yet we have ample means of controlling his account because of the rich apocryphal sources now known to the world. Each dispensation, which is a bestowal or handing down of the gospel by direct revelation, was immediately followed by general apostasy and wickedness.

Joel Erik Myres was married to a granddaughter of Hugh Nibley, Natalie Mincek. Myres passed away on 7 March 2001; this address was read at the graveside service held on 20 March 2001.

Adam and Eve “mourned before the Lord” (Moses 5:27), while their own offspring “loved Satan more than God” (Moses 5:18, 28). Enoch wept and “refuse[d] to be comforted” (Moses 7:44); he left the world with his righteous Zion to consign “the residue of the people” (Moses 7:20) to destruction. The flood had barely dried up when the people of Noah’s day were back to their old ways, “And the Lord said in his heart, I will not again curse the ground any more for man’s sake; for the imagination of man’s heart is evil from his youth” (Genesis 8:21). Abraham’s own parents turned from the gospel to the practice of idolatry and human sacrifice (Abraham 1:5–7), while his people went directly into four hundred years of bondage in Egypt, which they deserved. Hear Moses’ farewell address: “Behold, while I am yet alive with you this day, ye have been rebellious against the Lord; and how much more after my death?” (Deuteronomy 31:27).

Christ in his prophecies to the apostles predicted the destruction of Jerusalem and the scattering of the Jews as but “the beginning of sorrows” (Matthew 24:8)—two thousand years of them: “For then shall be great tribulation, such as was not since the beginning of the world to this time, no, nor ever shall be” (Matthew 24:21). And finally, Joseph Smith was shown a vision of the last days so terrible that he begged the Lord to take it away.

As far back as the record goes, people have been edified by great temple dramas depicting the creation, the council in heaven, Satan’s claim to complete control of the operation, and his expulsion from heaven. Joseph Smith has given us in the Book of Moses two such temple dramas, one starring Moses and the other Enoch.

The editors of the classical and Egyptian versions have been reminding us recently that the plays answer three main questions in their attempt to give majesty and purpose to the suffering of man. (1) Why is this world such a mean place? As Hamlet puts it, “Weary, stale, flat and unprofitable,” “an unweeded garden that grows to seed”—“things rank and gross in nature possess it merely.”1 There was a saying in the German villages where I spent my mission, “Das Leben ist wie ein Kinderhemd, kurz und besuldelt” (Life is like a baby’s bib, short and messy). (2) Where is God when we need him? This had a special name with the ancients, aporia, “absence in emergency,” “unable to make it,” “out to lunch.” Joseph Smith in Liberty Jail cries out, “O God, . . . where is . . . thy hiding place?” (D&C 121:1). And the Lord in Gethsemane pleads for relief, but knows he must go on to Golgotha. (3) Why, with our vast potential and talents, is our life so ridiculously short? Even genius takes time to develop. This is Omar Khayyám’s complaint: “One moment in annihilation’s waste, One moment of the well of life to taste— the stars are setting and the caravan starts for the dawn of nothing—O, make haste!”2 If you are being severely tested, why not be given a decent chance to show what you can really do?

These questions answer themselves. Don’t tell me there is no answer—if there was no answer there would be no question. But the answers are very clear, especially in Joel’s case. Almost everything that happens to us in this life presents us with a sort of test, from crossing the street to reaching the moon; getting up in the morning and going to bed at night, the slightest exertion challenging inertia (Newton’s First Law). Even more than our actions, our very thoughts are an unceasing test. Experts on the brain tell us that, unlike God, we can think of only one thing at a time; every individual must decide for himself at every moment just what he is going to think about.

So we are being tested here, and there is no escape from it. To recapitulate, Karl Popper has explained that to test anything, whether a piece of machinery, building materials, a student’s knowledge, or a weightlifter’s strength, it must be tried to the limit. Anything that stops short of that limit leaves the question in doubt. We need only think of Gethsemane. So don’t expect an easy test. As to aporia, God stands on the sidelines and does not interfere. Again we think of Gethsemane; angels brought comfort but no escape from the cross.

The severest test ever given to a human was Abraham’s, the sacrifice of his only son. And after the trial was over, he named the place Jehovah-jireh (Genesis 22:14), which means that God was watching all the time and knew exactly how it would turn out. Abraham may have known too because he told the men watching the pack animals that he and Isaac would be back in a little while. But Isaac did not know. For him it was the Akedah—Gethsemane. But Abraham, like all who would become children of Abraham, was tested right up to the point where the angel cried, “Lay not thine hand upon the lad, . . . for now I know that thou fearest God, seeing thou hast not withheld thy son, thine only son from me” (Genesis 22:12).

And why is the time so short? It takes years to acquire the knowledge, wisdom, skill, understanding, and experience necessary to achieve our maximum in this world. So after years of preparation one comes primed and ready to take the examination. A four-hour test would be a joyful opportunity—a chance to show off. But what happens? After five minutes the monitor says, “Time’s up, drop your pens and hand in your papers.” That is what happened to Joel. He worked like a demon and was prepared to write a ten-hour examination at least. But what happens? As I lie in bed I look at a solid wall of books by my side, chosen over a lifetime with the greatest care—they put me into the picture of the ancient world as efficiently and pleasantly as anything else could—and yet I have to turn over, fall asleep, and dismiss them with a sigh—I have them at last, but no time left to read them.

Isn’t the explanation to all three questions obvious?

(1) The routine presented by a vicious and contrary world?

(2) The absence of any ready assistance?

(3) The grossly disappointing examination that nobody is going to read? And at the end? What are we being tested for if not for achievement or ability? What then is all this testing about? The answer is that all this is simply to show what kind of people we are. Obviously we are being groomed for an immensely important assignment and for a vast expanse of time, and possibly for vicious and insidious opposition.

Have we the qualities to stand up to it? Because the next step we take will be for eternity. The covenants we make explain it. For what specifically are we being tested? What are the qualities and talents we will need for an eternal task of dealing with the highest and the lowest forms of existence? They are characteristics well-known to all of us. (1) First of all, in being willing to take the test, we show obedience, trusting our welfare to the wisdom of others. (2) And since this is going to require substantial yielding of our own sacro egoismo, that means sacrifice. (3) We are not alone and never will be, so we may never forget the rights and blessings of others which we share with them; the family is the model and sets the tone that prescribes our behavior; we are dealing always with others as dear to us as our own existence, so there can be no clashing of wills and temperaments. Such is the law of the gospel.

(4) Naturally, the wanton and excessive excitement characteristic of Roman society, and our own, has no place in a world where all things are sacred. We accept the law of chastity. (5) The whole thing is summed up in life in the world of the living—we all have the same feelings of justice and righteousness, devoid of legal quibbling or sophistry— in other words, perfectly spontaneous love for everything that is “good, true, and beautiful.” It is for all of us to give and to receive. That is the law of consecration.

Joseph Smith, the founder of this last dispensation, was the only mortal aware of just what is going on. He regretted the impossibility of communicating all he had seen. The question remains—Is this all there is? Here I am free to speak. From reasons based on the evidence of things around us and above us, I believe that Joel is joyfully employed at present. My reasoning is not perfect, but I can support it with a number of personal experiences that leave me in absolutely no doubt at all that Joel is engaged in a higher work. I expect to have this assertion confirmed in my own case before very long.

That we may comprehend the wisdom and love in God’s dealings with all of us is my prayer in the name of Jesus Christ. Amen.

Notes

^1. William Shakespeare, Hamlet, act 1, scene 2, lines 133–38.

^2. Rubáiyát of Omar Khayyám, 38, trans. Edward Fitzgerald (New York: Grosset and Dunlap, 1946).

Part 6

Temples

27

The Greatness of Egypt

This address was delivered on 12 March 1986 as part of the Ramses II International Lecture Series.

As to the subject of my talk today, I have chosen the safest title. What fifth-grade child after his class has visited the Ramses exhibit would be at a loss to produce a juvenile composition on “The Greatness of Egypt”? In fact, all over the valley these days school children, including my own grandchildren, are busily composing reports and preparing projects on that exotic culture. I hope to show before I’m through that the title is not as naive as it sounds—though I often wish we would declare a moratorium on that overworked word, great. Not being an Egyptologist, I welcomed this assignment as an inducement and an excuse for reviewing some twenty-five years of looking at inscriptions and papyri on my own, made possible by the blessing of Xerox and now expedited by my own aging notes and cribbings—and all that to decide whether I have learned anything or not.

Most Egyptologists have told us wherein they think the greatness of Egypt lies, though on some points they disagreed. Like the priest Chryses in the Iliad, I shall need some defense here, for I fear that what I shall say may offend the great ones.

But on some points all will concede the greatness of Egypt. First of all, there is the sheer mass and charge of the ruins, the overall grandeur of the show, as undeniable as the Grand Canyon. In my first year in high school I learned from James H. Breasted’s Ancient History that eighty-five men could stand atop one of the great pillars of the Hypostyle Hall at Karnak, and though I would not urge that many to try it, no further demonstration was needed to fix for all time in juvenile minds the noble dimensions of that civilization. But no less impressive than the bulk of the monuments was their age. If size times age is the equation of greatness, then the pyramids are in a class by themselves, looming vast and distant, the manifest reality of another world. But “That’s not it,” as Cleopatra says; size and age are relative things—the mountains just behind us are far bigger and older than man-made monuments. What is most important, after all, is style—the Egyptians had class, and they had it like no other people. (Don’t panic—we’ll get to the Greeks!) “There is something in every Egyptian work of art,” wrote Heinrich Schäfer, “that links it to every other Egyptian work of art, and distinguishes it from every non-Egyptian work of art.”1 “Why is it,” asks another eminent Egyptologist, “that a traveler will believe things in Egypt that he will not believe outside of Egypt?” And he answers: “It is because a singular and awesome sense of the eternal goes over the whole land.”2 The unmistakable and inimitable Egyptian style, writes another, is “characterized by a harmony between grace and majesty. . . . The permanency of this style is the most striking feature of the ancient Egyptian culture. This proves that the ancient Egyptian culture was animated by great inner strength. It was entirely homogeneous,”3 or at least, we would add, original and unique, as is also the matchless written heritage. “For vividness of expression and richness in human touches,” Alan H. Gardiner observed, “the Egyptian records vastly excel their counterparts from other ancient Oriental lands.”4 Some visitors to such exhibits as the Ramses II see the characteristic stamp of the culture in its funereal orientation; don’t blame the Egyptians for that, for as Gardiner assures us, “nine-tenths of all Egyptian excavations have been conducted on the high desert. . . . To this circumstance is due the overwhelming funerary character of most of the finds.”5 If you do all your digging in a cemetery you will find sundry products of the mortuary industry, but don’t complain that the people who put them there never thought of anything else.

There is plenty of evidence that the ancients felt the same awe and wonder before the Egyptian phenomenon that we do. Its grandeur, its stability, and its mystery are not imagined qualities. The ruins speak for themselves, but the Egyptians deserve special attention because they have gone out of their way to tell us about themselves. They have staked out their claim to primacy by leaving written clues on all sorts of things, strewn all along the trail from the beginning, often with urgent invitations to read and respond, to know who they were and what they did, and to profit by their experience. Many autobiographies (a convenient collection in Kurt Sethe’s Urkunden) give instructions on how to live—the seboyet (sbȝ.yt) of the schools, a Wisdom literature that is both frank and ethical, instructions of kings to their ministers and fathers to sons, funerary texts setting forth the qualifications for a happy hereafter, temple texts specifying the steps by which eternity is achieved, hymns of praise and contemplative as well as skeptical compositions, and romantic and exciting tales which on closer study reveal a deeper import than first meets the eye—all invite us to intimate acquaintance with these people. Even the cryptography is a challenge to the passerby to use his wits and satisfy his curiosity. By these we can take measure of the Egyptian character. Moreover, the evidence of their words is supported by that of their works—their monuments and sacred structures, their revealing portraits and domestic scenes of life at court or in the home, even the doings in the workshop and in the field projected in lifelike drawings and figurines, put us into the pictures.

From all of these the observer receives a strong impression of the unique and unmistakable Egyptian character. From the first they liked to attribute it to the landscape, in which, as Hermann Kees puts it, “everything emphasizes the constant and unchanging,” imparting a sense of “calm and regularity which unavoidably affected the character of the people.”6 The constancy and permanence of things is noted by everyone. “The motifs which already prevailed in the Old Kingdom, are still to be found in the texts and in the representations of the temples of the Ptolemaic period. This is positive proof that the typically Egyptian ideas remained constant.”7 The work of Walter B. Emery shows how everything appears suddenly full-blown about 3400 and stays that way. Thus the Book of the Dead quotes texts from the First Dynasty, and a splendid portrait bronze of the Thirty-first Dynasty shows us a man who sat for the artist two thousand years before.8 Portraits from the Twenty-fifth and Twenty-sixth Dynasties are easily mistaken for works of the Fourth or Fifth,9 just as the funeral text of those late periods could have come right out of the Pyramid Texts. “The Egyptians escape death by arresting time,” is Siegfried Morenz’s conclusion, and he cites Wolf: “No Egyptian picture indicates any time of day; . . . everything simply is, nothing is becoming, everywhere we see rigid, timeless existence.”10

How then does one account for the ubiquitous appearance of the familiar kheper-scarab (ḫpr) who stands for transformation, the very symbol of change? “The Egyptian lives in a world of ceaseless ‘transformations’ (kheperou),” writes Max Guilmot.11 Change cannot be denied: it may seem like a great paradox or mystery to be constantly changing yet always remaining the same, yet the fact is that we see it all around us every day; the most obvious and undeniable fact of life is that each man in his time plays many parts, while all the time remaining himself. This coincidence of opposites is one of the most conspicuous concerns of the Egyptians: temples and tombs are not dead ends but places of passage in which one moves ever from chamber to chamber and from level to level—and then on to the beyond.12 This meant that the Egyptian could accept the reality of time and its ravages without being terrified by it—time moves us out of one condition only to promote us to another; no need for “lives of quiet desperation.” “One might ask,” writes John A. Wilson, “how a rigid and static order could possibly stand the wear and tear of change over so many centuries. . . . A tolerant and easygoing people could not be categorically absolute. . . . In art, for example, . . . there was always variety and individuality,” while “the dogma was of general nature, and the actual practice was flexible, tolerant, catholic, and pragmatic.”13 The Egyptian could afford the luxury of relaxing and enjoying a life that counted on a happy ending.

Every Egyptologist has commented on the ready acceptance of contradictions as the special mark of Egyptian thinking. In the cults and doctrines, Adolf Erman notes, “competition and conflict bothered no one,” yet in thought and action the practitioners were “calm and rational.”14 The prominent feature of Egyptian religion is that mythological concepts are very often contradictory, notes Rudolf Anthes, and explains: the Christian says, “It is so and therefore cannot be otherwise,” while the Egyptian says, “We can understand it so and also otherwise,”15 much in the manner of modern quantum physics. This is reflected in the dualism which is so conspicuous in the royal appointments of Egypt—two kingdoms, two thrones, two palaces, two tombs, two lands, etc., are existing at all times side by side, one never putting the other down in total victory. The earliest documents of all, the famous Predynastic palettes, as Siegfried Schott studied them, “show an attempt of speculative wisdom to explain this world as an interplay of Joy and Strife,” a sort of “allegory on the way of the world.”16 The famous Shabako text, one of the oldest religious writings, tells us that this life is a time of probation in which all must make their choice between good and evil ways to determine what their hereafter shall be: the world is necessarily a place of opposites, as a scene of testing and choosing.17

Perhaps the most remarkable aspect of apparent contradiction is a society of individualists whose ideal is that of absolute monarchy. The Pharaoh is all-in-all, but that leaves everybody else free and equal. An unbridgeable gulf between divinity and mortality? As is well known, “funerary customs . . . were originally reserved for the king,” but right at the outset they “spread to members of the royal house,” and then on down “until finally every Egyptian secured the right of identifying himself in death with Osiris, the royal god of the dead.”18 In this open society “artisans would be recruited from the peasants; . . . superior artisans . . . would thus move into the aristocracy.”19 In the best period, according to Wilson, “the democratic theory . . . knew no class lines but extended down to the lowest peasant,” on the principle enunciated by the wise man Amenemope: God has made all men an equal in opportunity.20 The king is the Good Shepherd, and the purpose of his kingship and priesthood is to serve all the others. Thus in the Fifth Dynasty tomb of Ti we see “a kind herdsman has lifted onto his shoulder a frightened calf that is looking round for its mother. The other herdsman seems to be quieting another animal. . . . This pastoral mood” expresses the religious doctrine of “the sun-god, as a good shepherd [who] takes care of mankind . . . and as the Great Preserver, makes the plants and beasts flourish.”21 It was this concern of all for all that boded well for the survival of the culture, that “sense of responsibility towards nature,” as Philippe Derchain puts it, which forbade the Egyptian to destroy life in any form or to hold it in contempt.22 Pharaoh felt responsible for all his possessions and did not deal with them wantonly or wastefully;23 it was his concern, expressed in the royal tomb of Ramses IV, not to harm any living creature.24 And it is the frequent boast of individual autobiographies that the subject has never harmed any living thing. On the other hand, the official representative of all that is immoral to the Egyptian mind is Seth, the archetypal villain, brutal, mindless, acquisitive, the bully boy who destroys gardens and groves in his wanton campaigns of conquest. Unable to create or to beget offspring, he is obsessed only with the quest for power and gain.25 Of what does the Egyptian boast in his epitaph? That he helped the weak and was kind to the stranger, that he sustained the people of his town when times were bad, that everyone loved him, that he never showed partiality to the rich and powerful or to his personal friends, and that he was honored by his father, loved by his mother, adored by his brothers and sisters, and blessed by all the household and all the citizens of the community. Naturally the German professors promptly labeled this glatter Phrasenschwulst, hypocritical rhetoric.26 But the important thing is that it holds up to us the ideal man of the Egyptians, how a man wanted to be and be thought of, the qualities which society in every period praised and valued. The only kind of management that we can imagine as capable of getting the pyramids built was that “sneer of cold command” which the poet tagged on the Egyptians when very little was known about them.27 Monuments like the tombs of el-Bersheh and Petosiris tell us how the great works were done in a very different spirit; the markings on pyramid stones put there in happy competition by the work gangs—the Go-getters, the Scepter Gang, the Tough Gang, the North Gang, etc.—names expressing the lively sense of pride, piety, and fun that animated everybody engaged in the operation.28

The Greeks often praised the Egyptian wise men as the most holy, righteous, healthy, long-lived, and learned, and the people as the happiest, most sober, and most moral people on earth.29 They had their values right: “It is a good thing,” said the wise Merikare, “to work for the future and live an active life. . . . But life on earth passes; it is not very long. . . . So don’t seek after property; the possessions of a million men are nothing to the Lord of the Two Lands. A good man shall live forever.”30 When things go wrong they know the reason; a king wrote down in person the dictation of the prophet Neferrohu, who sounds exactly like the prophets of Israel: “Behold the great one is fallen. . . . Perished is this land. . . . Every man’s heart careth only for his own self. I will show thee the son as an enemy, the brother as a foe, a man slaying his father,” the blight of a “me” generation.31

As Erman notes, the Egyptians had no bloodthirsty gods, no ecstatic or orgiastic rites, “the ordinances were carried out in a calm and rational manner, and god was treated exactly like an exalted man.”32 If a peasant who had been robbed by a rascally estate manager could appeal clear up to the king, “the king himself is bound by obligations of loyalty,” as a recently found document describes it, mere office does not endow the holder with sanctity as in modern society, but even “an incompetent king was considered to be unworthy and ailing.”33 Pharaoh was the top of the pyramid, Wilson notes in a useful insight, “the only point of contact with the gods.”34 But where does the top, the pyramidion, end and the pyramid begin? They are all one, every level indispensable to the next to make the solidest of structures and to take us all up to heaven along the same celestial ramp and broadening sunbeam.

The stability and endurance of a culture used to be ascribed to the peculiar geography of Egypt, compared to a tube with only a narrow opening at either end, keeping the people in splendid isolation. But if Egypt was a tube, it was one that exerted powerful suction at either end as well as in the middle, for there was corn in Egypt. It was a mixture of all sorts of races,35 conquered by Libyan, Hyksos, Nubian, Persian, Greek, and Roman. But each conqueror at once aspired to become the true and legitimate Pharaoh with all the mystique and the trappings; if Graeca Capta educated its Roman captors, the throne of Pharaoh, like the legendary throne of Solomon, miraculously transformed almost every conqueror who sat on it, including the Greeks, into a zealous convert to the faith of Amon.

If one word sums up the Egyptian nobility of character, it is humanity. It is a peculiarity of the language, Hermann Grapow found, to speak of others in warm and intricate terms as parts of one’s body, while on the other hand no matter how angry an Egyptian was with another, he never likened a fellow human to any animal, as we so often do.36 Hundreds of pictures from every period show people as they wanted to be, as equals. There is the family group with no one member overshadowing another. A typical portrait of the Sixth Dynasty shows the midget Seneb sitting with great dignity on a stand which puts him on the level with his wife. The little man was no less than the director of the important royal weaving works and a priest of the dead for the great King Cheops, while “his wife was a member of the royal house. He was not shamed to have his physical peculiarities portrayed.”37 Along with this equality an invincible individualism is seen all over in the tomb paintings, where the artist insists on doing his own thing, “actuated by a perverse and antic impulse,” and yet satisfied with being himself, never clamoring for public recognition. Wilson finds it a “paradox—to the modern—that he was a creative individual and yet was content to remain essentially anonymous; . . . he found his reward through serving his society faithfully.”38 It is paradoxical to our minds that anyone should create anything without a thought of fame and fortune. Everywhere in the pictures and inscriptions we find people hard-working, conscientious, eager to please those below their station as well as those above—one Pharaoh instructs an official not to bow down to any executive above his own rank! He knows the difficulty when middle management takes over. Optimistic, kind and tolerant, content with optimum conditions—why should they change? On the face of it such people have a better chance than the rest of us of weathering the slings and arrows that afflict nations as well as individuals.

The Doctors Disapprove

But there was one fatal flaw in the Egyptian culture that has denied it the recognition of the learned as a major contributor to Western civilization. Our representative scholar here shall be Erman, often hailed as the father of modern Egyptology, and especially helpful for his invaluable autobiography. Erman hails the work of Georg Ebers as having rid the world once and for all of the notion that the Egyptians “were a peculiar people, . . . unchanged through thousands of years, . . . a race of pious worshippers who were interested only in temples and tombs.”39 Yet strike out the “only” and that is what they were. It was the insistence of the Egyptians on injecting into the wisdom of the East allusions to cult and retribution hereafter based on the idea of the immortality of man that vitiated the purity of the proper philosophy and ethics that came to them from the Semitic world, according to Raymond Weill.40 Erman opens his classic work on Egyptian religion with an apology: “One cannot ask anyone to have any real enthusiasm for this barbarism, this age-old accumulation of absurdities.”41 The myths were bad enough, but the attempts of the priests to build a theology or philosophy on them was for Erman simply an abomination— a horror (“uns ein Greuel”).42 Gardiner found the writings “intolerably arid,” “unmitigated rubbish.”43 The best he can say for Egyptian religion is that it is as “alluring as a will o’-the-wisp by reason of its mystery and even in spite of its absurdity.”44 While noting the general tendency to take “an unsympathetic and even patronizing attitude toward the myths and religious practices of Pharaonic times,” he warns emphatically against taking the stuff seriously: “Perhaps even more dangerous is a standpoint” according to which priestly writings, such as the Hymn to Sobek, were regarded with respectful awe “just because the Old Egyptians were undeniably a highly gifted and truly civilized people.”45 After a brief flash of true intelligence, in which at least one Egyptian recognized that “phenomena were purely physical and not a product of divine or demoniacal forces,”46 the nation lapsed into religion and “groped for what they knew had been treasure, but groped in vain,” and so they “never achieved profundity,”47 and we have “the terrifying emptiness of Herodotus’s picture of the Egyptian as the most ‘god-fearing’ of peoples,” “utterly empty of mind or heart (and so) . . . exposed to the invasion of other-worldliness, monasticism or apocalyptic expectation.”48 Religion is the villain. The ancient Sophists put the same brand of superstition on the Egyptians, and Plutarch and Iamblichus in reply both ask them the same question that Santillana does today—“how the devil the pyramids happened to get built in spite of the strange frame of mind their constructors were cursed with.”49

Whence this prejudice against Egyptian religion? In the eighteenth century it had been accepted practice to explain “the mythologies, the sacred writings, and the monuments . . . by a reference to the Bible.”50 Then when Samuel Birch translated the Book of the Dead in 1867, it was hailed as “the Bible of the Egyptians,” but the inadequacy of the title was soon apparent. When in 1880 the far older Pyramid Texts were discovered, the experts, with a sigh of relief, pushed the Book of the Dead aside, all the more eager to demote it because it had exercised an irresistible attraction to occultists, professional mystics, and crackpots who muddied the waters in which they waded and brought discredit on more disciplined study.51 The great Erman tells of a professor at Berlin who actually believed that some things in the Bible were confirmed by archaeological finds in Egypt and Babylonia: “Never before or since,” he declared, “have I ever found such a strange phenomenon among educated people.”52 The Bible was out, and as in other fields, its place was taken by the eagerly welcomed doctrine of evolution. It all began with the fantasy of simple people, the workings of the natural surroundings on the primitive mind. The explanation was inevitable: Egypt went through the same evolution that every religion of a noteworthy people passes through.53 At the turn of the century Peter L. Renouf announced that “the myths of Egypt, like those of all other nations, arose (it cannot too often be repeated) from the spontaneous and often homely utterances of men in presence of nature.”54 “That Egyptian mythology, like the Indo-European, is derived from natural phenomena, and that its principal deities are names of the Sun, has, I suppose, never been doubted by modern scholars.”55 Greek science could learn nothing from the sources on Egypt simply because the Orientals were all “primitive” and “unsystematic,” and that goes even for the Babylonian cosmology, which was nothing but the “purest mythology . . . devoid of any abstract speculation.”56 Professor Otto Neugebauer would smile at that today.

The recondite nature of Egyptology made it possible for the schools to enjoy an absolute authority, for Egyptologists all too easily overrate their own learning and practice all manner of dilettantism while demanding unquestioning acceptance by the public. Erman tells us how he and the great Richard Lepsius used to laugh together at the naiveté of Gaston Maspero, which showed “how easily one can become ‘celebrated’ in this field” since the public knows nothing about it.57 And then he goes right on to tell the gentle reader what an inadequate teacher Lepsius himself was with his limited knowledge and his formal and aloof air of professorial importance.58 He goes right down the list, cutting the great ones down to size: “No one is so irresponsible (leichtherzig) and so clumsy in judging antiquities as the men of science and the technical experts,” he wrote— but what could the public know about that?59 Erman dismisses Heinrich K. Brugsch as unscrupulous, frivolous, and poorly educated; Edouard Naville as unpardonably imaginative, his thinking warped by “a childish hatred against l’école de Berlin [the school of Berlin]”;60 Ebers as a popularizer who knew nothing about Egyptian syntax; and W. M. Flinders Petrie as one who was uncritical and did not know the language, etc.61 As to Erman himself, “Not a single hour passed in which I did not preach the ars nesciendi, ‘I don’t understand it,’ and ‘God only knows what the Egyptians were thinking of there,’ were my daily confessions.”62 The sorest point of all was Egyptian religion on which Erman produced the definitive textbook: “Religion is the Schmerzkind of Egyptology. For half a century I have wrestled with it, and how little certainty has come of it after all. Everyone still invents his own Egyptian religion.”63 Here he lists the main theories, each with some merit, none decisive, with the conclusion: “One might say, however we figure it, we figure wrong; the problem is simply insoluble.” “I have never been able to escape that verdict.”64 Then why bother with it?

Erman’s pupil Breasted brought the Berlin franchise to Chicago (as Gardiner did to Oxford), where, as William F. Albright tells it, he “chafed against the exclusion” of his field from the “charmed circle” of the sciences, to which he finally gained admission by demonstrating how the Pyramid and Coffin Texts make it possible to recognize the organic evolution of Egyptian thought.65

This was that “evolutionary bias,” which, as Henri Frankfort notes, colors Egyptology through the nineteenth and twentieth centuries,66 and it was simplistic: man’s destiny is to improve steadily and irresistibly; man “has raised himself by his own bootstraps.” This philosophical naturalism Breasted called “the new humanism,”67 in which, Albright notes, Egyptologists shared with anthropologists and social scientists a “common dislike for Christianity and history.”68 It was as exact scientists that the positivists of Berlin would come before the world; they would have no truck with speculation.
