The exciting Berlin of the 1920s simply appalled Erman by its willingness to explore and speculate; the statement that “such and such a thing might be possible,” which rendered the new physics so exciting with its sense of uncertainties and possibilities, was for Erman utterly counterproductive, the very antithesis of science, which deals with absolute fact and nothing else.69 Such freedom of thought threatened to bring back “everything that we thought we had long since overcome and forgotten,” including such borderline disciplines as “Naturphilosophie and Mystik, and, I am ashamed to say, even the occult.” A feeling of physical nausea came over him, he says, when he was invited to lecture on graphology.70 As late as 1950 Weill insists that all speculations about the pyramids “should be killed by silence. They were built simply, soberly, and sanely; the science of their construction nous la connaissons positivment [we know without any doubt].”71 As Alfred Whitehead saw it, the scientific world was “suffering from a bad attack of muddle-headed positivism,” which taught “that there is the routine described in physical and chemical formulae, and that in the process of nature there is nothing else.”72 More significant for our theme, Erman eschewed the expansive and rewarding comparative methods of a Mommsen, Diels, and Harnack for rigid confinement to “hard work in my own speciality, the one thing I could do and wanted to do.”73

Breasted’s pupil and successor at Chicago was John A. Wilson. “We must have confidence,” Wilson wrote, “that historians have done their honest and painstaking best in collecting the data reverently, sorting them conscientiously, . . . that the historians are learned and sound in method and attitude. . . . We need the assurance only that the historian has tried to encompass all the details possible.”74 If this sounds like a speech from a comedy by Molière or a solo from Gilbert and Sullivan, we are not entitled to laugh, for the right to an opinion can be held, Professor Wilson assures us, “only by a professional archeologist who carries a union card indorsed by a majority of his peers. . . . We archeologists are a small, jealously guarded group with a tendency to inbreed.”75 I emphasize the strong antireligious bias of the Berlin school because through the years it has enjoyed the exclusive right of judging Joseph Smith and the Book of Abraham. The world has got exactly the verdict it wanted regarding that purported autobiography of the patriarch from a jury convinced beforehand that the author never existed.

How does one presume to understand a religion totally alien to one’s own thinking? Erman tells us that from early childhood, religion and the Bible meant absolutely nothing to him.76 A deaf man with the help of a good library could conceivably compose a standard history of music; and a blind man by having the books read to him could publish a learned history of art; in the same way scholars who believe nothing can and do spend their days turning out learned articles on religion, though the very essence of religion is faith. How do they justify themselves? Very simply, they admonish us to think like Egyptians or think like primitives. The observer is always superior to the observed, and the great thinker should by the easiest of declensions fathom the lesser minds of those beneath him. Erman assures us that since Egyptian religion has little relationship to rational thought, what one must do is to feel like and act like an Egyptian.77 Anthropologists and Egyptologists, who were once children themselves, find it very easy to descend into the childish mental level of the people they study. Some deny altogether to the Egyptians the capacity for rational thought; Louis Speleers insists that their brains are diseased. “This literature was created for and by brains that do not function as ours do. . . . It is as if the [Aristotelian] principle of contradiction: idem non potest simul esse et non esse, did not exist for them,”78 for whom “everything is as impoverished and material and concrete as the Christian metaphysics is abstract and spiritual.”79 Günther Roeder, who collected and translated all of the major religious texts exonerates even the Babylonians, Hindus, and Chinese from straying as far from reality as did the Egyptians.80 The doctrine of a thought-world incommensurate with our own was dignified in the 1930s and 1940s by the theory of a mythopoeic (or, for that matter, prelogical or magical) mode of thinking, which Eberhard Otto now assures us must be abandoned.81

The Changing Scene

Being so well dug in, the schoolmen are characteristically resistant to change, and many of them have lamented that so little progress has been made through the years in a science so richly endowed with means and materials. Erman recalls how when he generously supplied Eduard Meyer with new and relevant materials to their studies, “I was surprised that he was not in the least bit interested by it as I thought he would be. I have often thought of this when I have received new material after finishing a study.”82 It is, to say the least, annoying to have to tear up and realign our carefully laid plumbing when something new turns up, and scientists and all scholars, however they may claim that the search for truth at all costs is their only delight, do not always welcome change gladly. There was, for example, a definite loss of ground after World War I, Klaus Koch observes, when the broad comparative Oriental researches of former years were relinquished in favor of narrowly specialized and hence easier studies; and it was only a flood of archaeological and manuscript discoveries that got things moving again.83

The redoing of old texts, which makes up a large part of Egyptology, is bound to give second thoughts to successive generations, and since the midcentury there has been a growing tendency to self-doubt and dissension, as Kees, Bonnet, Morenz, Frankfort, Thausing, Zandee, and others found different answers to the question “What is the real essence and content of the Egyptian religion?”84 And while some have held that “the moment has not yet come to attempt the interpretation of Egyptian religion in depth; we are still gathering facts”; others, such as “A. M. Blackman,

J. H. Breasted, A. Moret, and to a certain extent K. Sethe,” were convinced that at least the main ideas could be discerned among the confusion of the documents,85 Erman having insisted that it is possible in spite of the tangled evidence to identify the Durchschnittsglauben or common faith of the Egyptians.86 Professor I. E. S. Edwards wrote in his famous book that “any attempt to reconstruct the historical and religious background of the Pyramids is fraught with hazards” and compares this problem to a jigsaw puzzle: “How often it occurs that some unexpected term in the pattern results in the necessity for a readjustment in the solver’s preconceived idea of the relationship between the various units!”87 In 1934 T. E. Peet confessed: “One is often asked this very simple question: Is it possible to read a piece of Egyptian as easily, as quickly, and as certainly as a piece of Greek or Latin? The answer to this must certainly be No. . . . Texts often present continuous difficulties with which those of a Platonic dialogue or even an Aeschylean chorus are hardly to be compared.”88 As Roeder sees it, the science got off on the wrong foot since “the earliest Egyptologists were in phonetic matters on the wrong trail, and so from the beginning have rendered the foundations of our science more or less flawed (etwas kraenklich).”89 “It must never be forgotten,” wrote Gardiner, “that we are dealing with a civilization thousands of years old and one of which only tiny remnants have survived. What is proudly advertised as Egyptian history is merely a collection of rags and tatters.”90 In the important Amduat texts, Derchain now finds it “almost impossible to understand what the Egyptians were trying to convey,”91 and of the important chapter 78 of the Book of the Dead, Adriaan de Buck wrote: “It is difficult to suppress a feeling of scepticism as to the intelligibility of the [Book of the Dead] version, not so much of its separate sentences, which as a rule are not difficult to translate, but above all things of the plot and story of the spell as a whole.”92 As to reading the Pyramid Texts, Raymond Faulkner quotes Gardiner: “The only basis we have for preferring one rendering to another, when once the exigencies of grammar and dictionary have been satisfied—and these leave a large margin for divergencies—is an intuitive appreciation of the trend of the ancient writer’s mind.”93 Even more important is George A. Reisner’s verdict on his extensive excavations of the tombs: “Our knowledge of the Egyptian conception of life after death is still very vague. We know that the utensils and weapons and ornaments of daily life were originally placed in the burial-chamber; . . . but we still have no exact knowledge of the details of the Egyptian conception of life after death.” The stock explanation, as we all know, was that the grave was naively conceived of as a house in which the dead went on living, supplied with all the ordinary necessities of life, but Reisner assures us, “Certainly we look in vain for a tomb which in form and plan represents a plausible reproduction of a living-house.” Instead what we find are structures “all planned to satisfy functions quite different from those of an ordinary house.”94 In short, from the Coffin Texts on, no one, according to Kees, “can get a clear picture of the Egyptian expectations of the hereafter.”95 I have strung out these annoying little details instead of confining them to the decent obscurity of a footnote in hopes of somewhat relaxing the stranglehold of the nineteenth-century positivism on some of our LDS intellectuals. This would not be necessary if they would consider some present-day trends in Egyptology.

“Quite recently,” Derchain writes, “scholars are participating in a veritable revival of Egyptian religious studies,”96 which necessarily entails taking the religion more seriously than the Old School ever did. Moreover, “recent criticism of the modern positivist attitude towards artistic and social evolution is applicable to Egypt.”97 Of prime importance is the note that the farther Egyptology progresses, the more reliable the accounts of the classical writers about Egypt appear.98 It used to be the other way around; it was unanimously agreed that the Greeks reporting on Egypt had been badly taken in by the ever-present self-appointed guides and rascally dragomans whom the learned visitors mistook for priests. The ordinary person is still at risk, wrote Meyer, so that “only an expert escapes turning the gorgeous East into a land of utter fantasy.”99

An important step has been the recognition “that many of our ideas about the early mythology are not well founded.”100 In fact, writes Anthes, “we have not yet a clear understanding of Egyptian myths.”101 Since the days of Max Mueller and the Grimms, mythology has been accepted as the natural foundation of ancient religious study. Since then, “Gardiner has pointed out that Egyptian mythological narratives are extremely rare, . . . derived from rather vague and fragmentary allusions. . . . Not a few of these texts are cryptic and unintelligible. For complete accounts of myths, we must as a rule turn to the Greek authors.”102 Eberhard Otto has shown that “the world of the Coffin Texts is primarily not a world of myth,” as has always been thought, but is “broader, more pristine, and more rich and undogmatic”—that is, a far more substantial offering than was ever supposed.”103 Constant resort to the much overworked and never defined realm of the “primitive” will no longer pass as an explanation. “The primitive in everyday life thinks exactly as we do,” Werner Kaiser pointed out, and there is no evidence that the prehistoric Egyptians ever went beyond the bounds of logical thinking.104 And Anthes concludes that “the unsophisticated so-called primitives are perhaps not primitives at all in the history of religion,”105 a lesson which Claude Levi-Strauss is now bringing home to the anthropologists and historians. Another trouble-saving word, the once ubiquitous magic, has also taken a back seat—though it is still there, its role in Egyptian religion is all but negligible in comparison with concern for the realities of cosmology.106 The predominance of myth in the interpretation of the Egyptian and other religions has been superseded by that of ritual; it was Schott who showed that the oldest religious texts are ritual and not mythological,107 even though “myth and ritual are branches of a single tree.”108 The myths usually attempt to explain the ordinances and not the other way around: “Recent studies of the earliest documents of Egyptian religion have revealed that mythical ideas grew out of the ceremonies; . . . the origin of the main myths can be traced back to the rites centering on the idea of divine kingship as established around 3000 B.C.”109 The relationship between Egyptian texts and pictures has always been puzzling. Svein Bjerke finds that the pictures show real people performing ordinances, while the accompanying vignettes report the event in terms of the original model; therefore, the participating mortals in the pictures are identified in the texts with Horus and Osiris in the act of initiating the original ceremony.110 Thus Gustave Jéquier now confirms what Gardiner showed long ago, that the wab-rite is really and truly a baptism by water.111

Another significant development is that those compositions which Erman valued as the only really precious Egyptian literature, the product of pure literary fancy and popular imagination, untainted by serious religious concern (he cites the Westcar Papyrus), are now being examined for their deeper eschatological content.112 At the same time there is a strong suspicion that the richly symbolic art of Egypt should be telling us much more than it has been. Though we call it symbolic art, we are almost totally ignorant of the thinking of those who created it.

But the most arresting development of our time has certainly been the final recognition of a real association of the Egyptian with the Hebrew literature over a long period of time, especially in the category of Wisdom literature. As far back as 1857 François J. Chabas remarked on the close resemblance between the Egyptian Wisdom texts and the books of Proverbs, Job, Isaiah, Psalms, Ecclesiastes, Wisdom, etc., but naturally concluded that these were only “sentiments natural to humanity” since of course there could be no possible connection between them. Still, he remained puzzled at finding that certain promises to the righteous were word for word the same in Ptahotep and the Decalogue.113 All along, Budge, Gressmann, Erman, Sethe, and others noted curious analogies between the Wisdom literature of the Bible and the Egyptian gnomic texts, but bravely refused to draw the obvious conclusions.114 When Gustave Lefebvre listed side by side identical statements from the tomb of Petosiris and the Bible, he marveled at the “exact parallelism of the words, the phrases, and the thought,” but hastily explained away the resemblances as “probably a fortuitous meeting of expressions and ideas.”115 When the Wisdom of Ani with its strikingly biblical passages was discovered, it was given the deep freeze by Eugène Revillout in 1902, and as late as the 1920s Wiedemann, Erman, and Reinke made no mention of the parallels in their manuals.116 In 1923 E. A. Wallis Budge suspected that the Wisdom of Solomon (e.g., Proverbs 15, 22, 23) might have come from the Wisdom of Amenemope, and in the following year Er-man actually proved it.117 It is significant that Erman was reluctant to exploit the full significance of his own discoveries of parallels between the Old Testament and Egyptian writings, which simply did not fit into the Approved School Solution.118 But since 1929 “there has never been any doubt about the influence of Egyptian wisdom on that of Israel,” in which Israel “has its closest contacts with Egypt” in “courtly wisdom.”119 Especially since World War II “an avalanche of commentaries and studies on the wisdom books” is emphasizing “the indubitable parallels between wisdom and the Egyptian Maʿat.”120 The broader search is back again; it is recognized that both Hebrew and Egyptian literature show “a certain universalism . . . common to these peoples of the Fertile Crescent. . . . Scholars have come to assume that the original setting for Israelite wisdom was the royal court” of Egypt.121 Need we recall that it was at the court of Egypt that Abraham exchanged wisdom with the natives, according to the Book of Abraham? Significantly, the old view that Egyptian Wisdom literature was almost totally utilitarian has happily been laid to rest for many years now.122

Beyond the Wisdom literature, the “great strides . . . and growing body of texts” extend to resemblances to the prophetic literature of Israel, such as passages in the Eloquent Peasant.123

Beyond Appearances

The size, manifest age, and splendor of the Egyptian show is after all only a façade. Greatness is not to be achieved by special effects; the evidences of power and wealth and the ceremonial patriotism of our own day “wherein doth sit the dread and awe of kings,” are at best indications of greatness, but it is not greatness itself. The Lord, if we will recall, uses the figure of the “whited wall” that looks impressively chaste and proper—but what is behind it? We have all seen how the sets at Universal City crumble and collapse after a few years. But the Egyptian façade does not crumble and collapse, though subjected from time to time to appalling forces of destruction such as have wiped out other civilizations. There must be something behind it, and we think we know what it is.

First, let us remember that Egyptian culture is a unified and monolithic religious structure. From the earliest to the latest, the writers of the sacred books “all assumed the existence of the same beliefs in their readers”—Erman’s Durchschnittsreligion.124 What gave the Egyptians their strength? It is religion, but more than that it is one particular doctrine, a gospel in the proper sense of the word; it is what Alexandre Moret called “a revelation concealed by hieroglyphics.”125 In Egypt alone we see the same teachings handed down, adapted but unaltered from the earliest time to the latest in the well-known procession from the Shabako text to the Pyramid Texts to the Coffin Texts, Book of the Dead, Books of Gates and Hours, Book of Breathings, and beyond.126 Hans Bonnet, who compiled the Encyclopedia of Egyptian Religion, chides the others for not “enquiring after the inner content and religious thrust (Ertrag)” of things and asks whether at this late date we ever really can know what was in the minds of those strange people.127 Today we would approach the problem by the ubiquitous questionnaire or poll. With the wealth of clues at our disposal we can ask the Egyptians themselves what they are getting at. It is their symbols that give them away. Everywhere we look we see them; the Egyptians seemed positively obsessed with them. And what is their order of priority; what really concerns the Egyptians most?

Life, the ankh-symbol, easily comes first. And since life is everything desirable, its curtailment is more than anything else to be avoided—what inscription or ceremony does not end with the signs for time and eternity—neheh (nḥḥ) with its everlasting circle and ḏjet (ḏ.t) guaranteeing not merely a spiritual survival, but a solid reality? Consistent with this, the next most common symbols are those that always follow the king’s name: after ankh (ʿnḫ) comes wedja (wḏȝ) and seneb (snb)—not only an everlasting and real existence, but one with all organs, parts, and dimensions completely restored and in perfect running condition. Next the was-scepter of divine authority, the ḏjed-pillar (ḏd) of ever-renewed stability, and, of course, the endlessly discussed Maat, the assurance that balance, justice, righteousness—in a word, truth— must prevail.

If life is the issue, there is one question that supersedes all others—the big question and in the end the only question. In view of the fact that “concern for the dead is the most outstanding characteristic of the Egyptians,” Kees observed, “it is most strange that students never ask why this should be so.”128 The answer is self-evident, but modern man does not like it because he finds it infinitely depressing. “The greatest gift that God can bestow upon the children of men,” said Brigham Young, “is the gift of eternal life; that is, to give mankind power to preserve their identity—to preserve themselves before the Lord.”129 Preserve themselves, that is, as individuals, not as essences reabsorbed into a sea of being or reconciled by strenuous mental discipline to accepting nonexistence as a beatitude. The Egyptian wants to go on as himself. We see it in the Coffin Texts, the passionate desire to know all, escape all confinement, enter in among the enlightened ones and pass onward and upward as the lower world yields its bonds to the qualified and the informed.130 At all times Egyptian “religion seems to rest on unified concepts, with the individual phenomena subordinated to these as variations on a basic theme. . . . The creation, maintenance, and continuation of life beyond death, in the cosmos as well as on earth, are central to the religion of Ancient Egypt. . . . After creation . . . the vital force of all creatures has to be preserved for eternity, this being accomplished by a continuous renewal or rejuvenation.”131 “Eternal lives” is the theme.

And the Egyptians had a definite plan for achieving it, set forth in the medu-netcher (mdw-nṯr), the Words of God coming down from the beginning and from on high.132 When a great king said, “I would see god as he is, I would act according to the gods’ command, . . . I would act as a god acts,” he was instructed in reply to go to the library, “the House of Life,” and search the holy books in which he discovered, “I am his Son who shall save his Father; I am in his image; and . . . I teach you to know the true way of life”—the plan.133 “Don’t count on length of years,” said a great Egyptian teacher of the Old Kingdom, “for after all a lifetime is but an hour, . . . but eternal existence lies beyond. A fool may make light of it, but he who has reached it without wrongdoing shall continue yonder like a god.”134

We have to do with a specific doctrine and a specific course of action prescribed by it, requiring specific times and places of observation. Most enduring edifices in the world are temples and tombs, both places of passage and inseparably connected. The temple is what the Egyptologist William J. Murnane calls “a model of the world ‘writ small’”135 or, as we have often said, a scale model providing a place where you take your bearings on the universe; hence the orientation of the temples, ceilings covered with stars and star calendars on the walls (decans and zodiacs), obelisks and apertures to mark the passage of the sun, stairways, ramps and passages, etc. The tomb, like the temple, is now seen to be a place of passage which one enters “to be regenerated, but not for a permanent stay”; one must descend there to comprehend “the cosmos as a whole . . . as one elementary universal structure which is mythically presented in the form of a series of pictures” to provide the dead with a “compendium of all knowledge.”136 As in the temple, pictures and texts taken together “present a veritable system of the universe”;137 it is no longer the dead end, the place “to lie in cold obstruction and to rot,” but is a center of action. The Egyptian Book of the Dead, like the Tibetan one, is concerned particularly with the exact moment of passage from one world to the other, that fearful passage described by the persons interviewed in Dr. Raymond Moody’s well-known studies; the problem, as Erik Hornung expresses it, is to get as close to the Nahtstelle, the interface, as possible, when there is just a chance that we might get “a peek through a chink in the wall.”138

The three motifs that confront us wherever we turn in temple and tomb are eternity, family, and cosmos. As to family, the gods themselves do not appear in solitary splendor in the great temples, but always have the rest of the family along, as the individual in his tomb wants to be seen in the intimate and loving company of his wife and children; whether gods, kings, or commoners, they hold hands and embrace in an easy and affectionate manner. Of particular interest are those ordinances in the Coffin Texts “for sealing a man’s family to him in the next world, his father, his mother, his brothers, his sisters, servants and attendants”—all in whom he has an interest.139 Egypt itself was organized as a great family, “beginning with the father-son relationship and going up to the god-god relationship.”140

Real survival as an individual and family calls for a real world hereafter, a viable ambience; like the earliest Christian apologist, Aristides, the Egyptian was not willing to settle for allegories and abstractions. “Egyptian religion,” wrote Alexandre Piankoff, “is a physical science . . . strangely reminiscent of the ideas of Heraclitus of Ephesus.”141 It was not, says Derchain, “modern religion, but rather a cosmology, a true physical science,” a “véritable physique vitaliste,”142 in which entropy is always at work and must be countered: “by the mind (esprit) alone chaos is held at bay, and all collapses when the mind is withdrawn.”143 But like quantum physics, it was a physical science which went beyond the Baconian realm of the obvious, extending to a serious Wissenschaft vom Jenseits (science of the beyond) of the great age “but nonetheless creatively researched.”144 It reminds one of the so-called “anthropic principle” in modern physical science. The thought is reinforced and implemented by ordinances— words and symbolic actions. The two basic laws are (1) multiple emergences—that is, the idea that there are many valid ways of attacking a problem, and (2) the principle of proxy, especially with the relationship to sacrifice.

The most concise and ancient statement of the doctrine, found in the so-called Shabako text, is in the opinion of Sethe, Junker, Breasted, and others “the most instructive which we possess for the inner content of Egyptian religion.”145 By this document, “the impact of the Memphite Theology was so fundamental,” writes Louis V. Å½abkar, that its effect and influence on Egyptian religious thought remained constant until the end of the Egyptian religion.

Unparalleled in the history of the ancient Orient as far as its cosmogenic significance is concerned, it traveled from century to century, from one theological system to another; its theme resounds from the first line of Genesis, and from there on through the Old Testament and the latest period of Hebrew literature it reaches the pages of the New Testament, witnessing to what an extent its conception of the creative power of the Word of God persisted in the ancient Orient, becoming a universal theological theme.146

More than that, “the Memphite Theology, through the Old Testament, prepared . . . the consciousness of the religious world for John’s Logos doctrine about the manifestations and creative power of the Word of God, so the Amarna Theology sounded some themes which in the New Testament and especially in John’s Gospel represent the fundamental features of the relation between the Father and the Son.”147

Is that going too far? Do we really owe so much to a civilization that passed away so long ago? Wilson, doubting “whether anything vitally essential can be transmitted from one culture to another,”148 asks why the Greeks gave the Egyptians so much credit. He finds the answer in the façade: “It was impossible to visit Egypt without respectful awe before the mighty pyramids and vast temples,” and being taken in by that “air of mysterious profundity,” which still impresses everybody. But from the list of great names attached to testimonials of Egyptian Wisdom, this explanation seems, to say the least, superficial. Specifically, for Wilson, “the influence of Egypt did not shape the Hebrews and the Greeks. They were shaped by their own experiences and their own inner dynamism.”149 But the Hebraism and Hellenism that the schoolmen handed on to conventional Christianity and normative Judaism was that which came from the schools at Alexandria where the Egyptian spell was never missing. If Joseph the Hebrew in the celebrated legend of Joseph and Asenath became Pharaoh after marrying the daughter of the High Priest of Heliopolis (an event testified by Genesis 41:45, 50; 46:20), no less did Alexander the Greek base his legitimacy as the Cosmocrator of the age wholly on his being a true pharaoh, a son of Amon.

Alexandria was not a hostile enclave of intellectuals in the empty shell of a burnt-out civilization. It was founded by Alexander and built up by his successors, schools, temples and all, full of their calling as sons of Amon; they had displaced a dynasty founded by the Persian Darius I who was as dedicated to his Pharaonic calling as Alexander himself; in fact, his greatest work was the temple of Amon at the Khargah Oasis with its great Hymn to Amon. Cambyses’ stay in Egypt was a voyage of information to study the art of government in Egypt, which became for the Persians, as later for the Greeks, a veritable school of government. The caesars followed suit; every one of them received the title of Pharaoh, for which calling some displayed true enthusiasm.150 While the schoolmen have been unanimous in giving all credit to the Greeks and denying all to the Egyptians, the Greeks, who were not noted for their modesty, were just as unanimous in giving all credit to the Egyptians; and in reply the professors have been unanimous in declaring that those clever Greeks did not know what they were talking about and had merely been taken in by transparently phony Egyptian guides. It is a ridiculous situation, but it now becomes clear that the more we discover about the Egyptians, the more we realize that the Greeks were telling the truth after all. Here is a recent example: the first Ptolemy in founding his dynasty sought to be a sound, standard-model pharaoh and, following the example of the founders of other dynasties, brought the history of the land up-to-date, assigning the famous historian Hecataeus of Abdera to write a history showing that Egypt had been the “home of wisdom from time immemorial.”151 Now Professor Jaeger discovered that it was Hecataeus who first applied the term historia to human affairs,152 borrowing it from the medical vocabulary, where it signified the description of the progressive symptoms of a disease. It so happens that a newly found teaching from the Fifth Dynasty describes the state in just such medical terms. So it was an Egyptian idea after all.

If we list the names of ancient philosophers and scientists who supposedly studied in Egypt, we find that the common quality of their teachings is the inextricable intermingling of the sacred and profane. For these people there is no such thing as the strictly profane, though the nineteenth-century professors did their best to separate the categories. Can we admit that these Egyptians had the true religion? It is always possible to sound the buzzword pagan to put them in their place. Thus when Schott is impressed by the close resemblance of certain Middle Kingdom funeral rites to the Christian sacraments, he quickly extricates himself from embarrassing questions by observing that the sacraments are “of course something entirely new and original.”153 In the same way, the Dead Sea Scrolls, when they seem to threaten the originality of both Christianity and Judaism, are simply thrust aside, and Breasted was able to dismiss the Book of Abraham because Abraham the monotheist could have absolutely nothing to do with the polytheistic paganism of Egypt, a statement that sounds amusing today.

Along with the resemblances that disturb both the ministry and the Old School go connections—connections everywhere. Let us begin with the Bible. That same Ptolemy who wished to shore up his new dynasty with a history from the beginning was assured that the record would not be complete without the books of Moses, which went back to the creation, and so under his direction the Septuagint, still the oldest existing version of the Torah, was produced at Alexandria as a royal Egyptian project. In turn that became the official text of the Old Testament cited throughout the New Testament as we have it today.

And speaking of façades, as you wander about our national capital, you will see the Roman influence on all sides and wisely explain to the children that they really go back to the Greeks. But why stop there? On to Egypt and the oldest stone buildings in the world, to Saqqara, where we find rows of graceful, white, shining, fluted columns. Following the examples of classical historians, the art historians were a very long time in admitting that the first real classical sculpture, the Apollo Kouros figures, are dead ringers for Egyptian portrait statues. And as to the greatest monument of them all, the Washington Monument, what is it but an Egyptian obelisk, dedicated with a solemn display of Masonic Egyptianry?154

Which brings us to the Hermetic tradition. Hermes, the Egyptian Thoth, wrote the sum and substance of all wisdom in the beginning, and from ancient times until the present, individuals and groups, countless secret societies and conventicles—the best known of which is the Masonic Order—have attempted to trace their origin to Egypt. Whatever the true and pristine stream may have been, among the adherents of the tradition have been some of the greatest and most productive and most original minds in science and literature.

Turning to conventional Christianity, a few names deserve mention. The famous catechetical school of Alexandria, which ran parallel to its celebrated university, the Museion, was first headed in the second century by Pantaenus, who was sent by the Coptic Bishop of Alexandria to preach in India. The great Clement of Alexandria was next head of the school, followed by his even more illustrious pupil Origen, the true fountainhead of Christian theology from which St. Augustine drew most of his teachings. Note the name—Origen, Born of Horus. Hardly less are St. Augustine and Christian theology indebted to Neoplatonism for the substance of their teaching; that movement was founded by Ammonius Saccus—again, note the name, “He belongs to Amon.” The definitive statement of conventional Christian faith is the Nicene Creed composed by Athanasius, the metropolitan of Alexandria, while its great rival was the teaching of an equally fervid Egyptian, Arius. As if to prove his deep Egyptian roots, Athanasius for a while went to live in the desert with St. Anthony, a humble Egyptian peasant, who as the official founder of monasticism, was simply following in the footsteps of other pious Egyptians before him. His way of life became a discipline of world historical importance when it was properly organized with rules and regulations by St. Pachomius. Pachomius was a Roman—that is, a Byzantine soldier, who while cracking down on Egyptian villagers was so impressed by their gentle and charitable ways toward even the insolent soldiery that he became converted, followed the example of Anthony, and laid down the Rule of Pachomius under which the monks lived “in complete harmony and perfect discipline within a structure that looked like a vast Roman fortification,” writes Professor Aziz S. Atiya,155 though it looked even more like the busy and massively walled precinct of an Egyptian temple. The Rule of Pachomius and the Life of Anthony became the franchise of that Western monasticism to which we commonly attribute the preservation of letters and learning in the Western world through the Dark Ages and on into the Renaissance, though it should be mentioned that the monks of the order also went forth from Egypt to carry on their work in India and Ethiopia. But as a beginning student of European literature, starting with Anglo-Saxon, I was puzzled to find the earliest vernacular documents from Italy, Switzerland, Gaul, Ireland, and Britain to be the work of monks from the Thebaid—Upper Egypt, of all places. They seem to have given us everything we had.

But still the open-minded inquirer asks the question: Did the Egyptians really have something? And the answer is yes. Their greatness lies in their refusal to accept as closed a case which the officious constables, pompous judges, and the brain-washed public considers settled once for all. They were honest enough to recognize that the evidence is far from complete, and until it is, it is they, and not we, who remain open-minded.

An Egyptian expression for “everything” was netet yutet (nt.t -wt.t), meaning everything of which I am aware plus everything of which I am not aware, which is infinitely vaster. The Egyptian always makes allowance for that margin of possibilities which Erman deplored. Even the “apparently shocking contradictions, in their teaching, have a great usefulness,” as Jéquier notes, “since they show us that the final truth has not yet been found.”156 This, Karl Popper tells us, is the essence of the scientific attitude.

An important feature of Egyptian architecture of temple, tomb, and even palace is a door, sometimes shown as a curtain or lattice, through which a spirit can pass, a means of communication between two worlds; and the literature is full of ceremonial and mythical doors and gates and instructions on how to pass them. Now the sum and substance of modern wisdom is that there is nothing behind the door— there lies the emptiness, the world of Pascal’s libertine. “Existentialist nausea has found its home in the most affluent and technologically advanced parts of the world.”157 How do we know there is nothing behind that door? The Egyptians realized that they did not have the answer, but their temples were storehouses of the past from which nothing was ever thrown away; in Erman’s famous metaphor, the Egyptian always had some of the primal eggshell still sticking to him. What they did have was hints, clear indication that at some time men knew something that would make a great deal of difference to us if we knew it. It was these hints that kept them going, and today it is widely recognized that science itself is advanced by the thrust of a thousand hints. It is to the credit of the Egyptians, not to their shame, that they never gave up looking even when they were not sure.

“Never on earth,” wrote Meyer, “was such a strenuous and persistent attempt undertaken to make the impossible possible in spite of everything; . . . the Egyptian believed in deadly earnest on this possibility.”158 “The impression made on the modern mind,” writes Professor Edwards, “is that of a people searching in the dark for the key to truth, and having found not one but many keys resembling the pattern of the lock, retaining all less perchance the appropriate one should be discarded.”159

“Possibility,” “perchance”—those are the key words. A sense of possibility allows for one’s own ignorance, and it leaves the door open. Sir John Eccles, the great authority on the brain, speaking of a possible hereafter, writes, “The unknown future that might be ours could be the fulfillment of our present life; and if we cannot assert its inevitability, we can at least accept its possibility as the greatest of all gifts.”160 A mere possibility, the greatest of all gifts in the eyes of this Nobel Laureate.

Following Pascal’s lead, we can put the situation in the terms of a wager or a lottery. As long as there is any chance at all of winning what you desire more than anything else, why not take a chance, instead of pigheadedly insisting that the game is already lost because your winnings are not guaranteed? If you bet against the hereafter, said Pascal, and win, you win nothing; if you bet for it and win, you win everything. Either way you have no way of knowing how it will turn out, but even if the chances against it were a thousand to one, it would still be worth it to take the chance. It has always been held by the humanists that it is the hardheaded realities of science that force us to take our stand, in William James’s terms, “on the firm foundation of unyielding despair”; it is they and not the scientists who have sung from the beginning nos habebit humus; it is they and not the scientists who have insisted with relentless iteration on the nox perpetua una dormienda, that night of mere oblivion toward which all things are relentlessly moving.

The only question is whether there is really any chance of winning. To the Egyptian embraced by an almost celestial natural ambience, the answer was obvious: Look around you! Whatever made the world where you find yourself, even if it was only a series of accidents, has already proven itself quite capable of carrying out an infinitely complex series of operations which somehow accrue to your advantage, though men may not see, know, or even suspect its existence. The explosive exchanges, permutations, and combinations of natural forms and symbols that cover the walls of Egyptian tombs and temples express the conviction that all sorts of things are possible. As Whitehead puts it, “the religious insight is the grasp of this truth: That the order of the world, the depth of reality of the world, the value of the world in its whole and in its parts, the beauty of the world, the zest of life, the peace of life, and the mastery of evil, are all bound together—not accidentally but by reason of this truth: that the universe exhibits a creativity with infinite freedom, and a realm of forms with infinite possibilities.”161 Isn’t it just possible that there may be a place for you in that vast segment of reality the netet yutet (nt.t -wt.t), of which we in our present state know nothing? The Egyptian steps into a bog when he leaves this life, but we step into a bottomless pit; he may pass through mists of darkness but we go with Catullus into perpetual night; nor can we evade the Big Question by joining the fashionable crowd of sophisticates partying in “the great and spacious building.” The Egyptian message and example to us is the same as that of science and our own religion—keep looking! And therein lies their true greatness, that in spite of everything they never shut the door on other worlds.

Meanings and Functions of Temples

This subentry, under the entry Temples, was published in the Encyclopedia of Mormonism (New York: Macmillan, 1992), 4:1458–63; this appears to be an abbreviated version of Hugh Nibley, “What Is a Temple,” in Mormonism and Early Christianity, CWHN 4:355–90.

The temple is the primal central holy place dedicated to the worship of God and the perfecting of his covenant people. In the temple his faithful may enter into covenants with the Lord and call upon his holy name after the manner that he has ordained and in the pure and pristine manner restored and set apart from the world. The temple is built so as to represent the organizing principles of the universe. It is the school where mortals learn about these things. The temple is a model, a presentation in figurative terms, of the pattern and journey of life on earth. It is a stable model, which makes its comparison with other forms and traditions, including the more ancient ones, valid and instructive.

The Cosmic Plan

From earliest times, temples have been built as scale models of the universe. The first known mention of the Latin word templum is by Varro (116–27 B.C.), for whom it designated a building specially designed for interpreting signs in the heavens—a sort of observatory where one gets one’s bearings on the universe. The root tem-in Greek and Latin denotes a “cutting,” or intersection of two lines at right angles, and hence the place where the four regions of the world come together, ancient temples being carefully oriented to express “the idea of preestablished harmony between a celestial and a terrestrial image.”1 According to Varro, there are three temples: one in heaven, one on earth, and one beneath the earth.2 In the universal temple concept, these three are identical, one being built exactly over the other, with the earth temple in the middle of everything, representing “the pole of the heavens, around which all heavenly motions revolve, the knot that ties earth and heaven together, the seat of universal dominion.”3 Here the four cardinal directions meet, and here the three worlds make contact. Whether in the Old World or the New, the idea of the three vertical levels and four horizontal regions dominated the whole economy of such temples and of the societies they formed and guided.

The essentials of Solomon’s temple were not of pagan origin but a point of contact with the other world, presenting “rich cosmic symbolism which was largely lost in later Israelite and Jewish tradition.”4 The twelve oxen (1 Kings 7:23–26) represent the circle of the year, and the three stages of the great altar represent the three worlds. According to the Talmud, the temple at Jerusalem, like God’s throne and the law itself, existed before the foundations of the world.5 Its measurements were all sacred and prescribed, with strict rules about it facing the east.

Its nature as a cosmic center is vividly recalled in many passages of the Old Testament and in medieval representations of the city of Jerusalem and the Holy Sepulchre. These show the temple as the exact center, or navel, of the earth. It was in conscious imitation of both Jewish and Christian ideas that the Muslims conceived of the Kaʿba in Mecca as “not only the centre of the earth, [but] the centre of the universe. . . . Every heaven and every earth has its centre marked by a sanctuary as its navel.”6 What is bound on earth is bound in heaven. From the temple at Jerusalem went forth ideas and traditions that are found all over the Jewish, Christian, and Muslim worlds.

The Place of Contact

As the ritual center of the universe, the temple was anciently viewed as the one point on earth at which men and women could establish contact with higher spheres. The earliest temples were not, as once supposed, permanent dwelling places of divinity but were places at which humans at specific times attempted to make contact with the powers above. The temple was a building “which the gods trans-versed to pass from their celestial habitation to their earthly residence. . . . The ziggurat is thus nothing but a support for the edifice on top of it, and the stairway that leads . . . between the upper and lower worlds”;7 it resembled a mountain, for “the mountain itself was originally such a place of contact between this and the upper world.”8

Investigation of the oldest temples represented on prehistoric seals concludes that these structures were also “gigantic altars,” built both to attract the attention of the powers above (the burnt offering being a sort of smoke signal) and to provide “the stairways which the God, in answer to these prayers, used in order to descend to the earth, . . . bringing a renewal of life in all its forms.”9 From the first, it would seem, towers and steps for altars were built in the hope of establishing contact with heaven (Genesis 11:4).

At the same time, the temple is the place of meeting with the lower world and the one point at which passage between the two is possible. In the earliest Christian records, the gates and the keys are closely connected with the temple. Some scholars have noted that the keys of Peter (Matthew 16:19) can only be the keys of the temple, and many studies have demonstrated the identity of tomb, temple, and palace as the place where the powers of the other world are exercised for the eternal benefit of the human race.10 The gates of hell do not prevail against the one who holds these keys, however much the church on earth may suffer. Invariably temple rites are those of the ancestors, and the chief characters are the first parents of the race.11

The Ritual Drama

The pristine and original temple rites are dramatic repetitions of the events that marked the beginning of the world. This creation drama was not a simple one, for an indispensable part of the story is the ritual death and resurrection of the king, who represents the founder and first parent of the race, and his ultimate triumph over death as priest and king, followed by some form of hieros gamos, or ritual marriage, for the purpose of begetting the race. This now familiar year-drama is widely attested—in the Memphite theology of Egypt, in the Babylonian New Year’s rites, in the great secular celebration of the Romans, in the panegyris and beginnings of Greek drama, in the temple texts of Ras Shamra, and in the Celtic mythological cycles. These rites were performed “because the Divinity—the First Father of the Race—did so once in the beginning, and commanded us to do the same.”12

The temple drama is essentially a problem play, featuring a central combat, which may take various mimetic forms—games, races, sham battles, mummings, dances, or plays. The hero is temporarily beaten by the powers of darkness and overcome by death, but calling from the depths upon God, “he rises again and puts the false king, the false Messiah, to death.”13 This resurrection motif is essential to these rites, whose purpose is ultimate victory over death. These rites are repeated annually because the problem of evil and death persists for the human race.

Initiation

The individuals who toiled as pilgrims to reach the waters of life that flowed from the temple were not passive spectators. They came to obtain knowledge and regeneration, the personal attainment of eternal life and glory. This goal the individual attempted to achieve through purification (washing), initiation, and rejuvenation, which symbolize death, rebirth, and resurrection.

In Solomon’s temple, a large bronze font was used for ritual washings, and in the Second Temple period, people at Jerusalem spent much of their time in immersions and ablutions. Baptism is one specific ordinance always mentioned in connection with the temple. “When one is baptized one becomes a Christian,” writes Cyril, exactly as in Egypt by the same rite one becomes an Osiris14—that is, by initiation into immortality. The baptism in question is a washing rather than a baptism, since it is not by immersion. According to Cyril, this is followed by an anointing, making every candidate, as it were, a messiah. The anointing of the brow, face, ears, nose, breast, etc., represents “the clothing of the candidate in the protective panoply of the Holy Spirit,” which however does not hinder the initiate from receiving a real garment on the occasion.15 Furthermore, according to Cyril, the candidate was reminded that the whole ordinance is “in imitation of the sufferings of Christ,” in which “we suffer without pain by mere imitation his receiving of the nails in his hands and feet: the antitype of Christ’s sufferings.”16 The Jews once taught that Michael and Gabriel will lead all the sinners up out of the lower world: “they will wash and anoint them, healing them of their wounds of hell, and clothe them with beautiful pure garments and bring them into the presence of God.”17

Loss of the Temple Ordinances

The understanding of the temple and its ancient rites was eventually corrupted and lost for several reasons. Both Jews and Christians suffered greatly at the hands of their enemies because of the secrecy of their rites, which they steadfastly refused to discuss or divulge because of their sanctity. This caused misunderstanding and opened the door to unbridled fraud: Gnostic sects claimed to have the lost rites and ordinances of the apostles and patriarchs of old. Splinter groups and factions arose. A common cause of schism, among both Jews and Christians, was the claim of a particular group that it alone still possessed the mysteries of God.

The rites became the object of various schools of interpretation. Indeed, mythology is largely an attempt to explain the origin and meaning of rituals that people no longer understand. For example, the Talmud tells of a pious Jew who left Jerusalem in disgust, wondering, “What answer will the Israelites give to Elijah when he comes?” since the scholars did not agree on the rites of the temple.18

Ritual elements were widely copied and usurped. The early Christian fathers claimed that pagan counterparts had been stolen from older legitimate sources, and virtually every major mythology tells of a great usurper who rules the world.

Comparative studies have discovered a common pattern in all ancient religions and have traced processes of diffusion that spread ideas throughout the world. The task of reconstructing the original prototype from the scattered fragments has been a long and laborious one, and it is far from complete, but an unmistakable pattern emerges.19

Reconstructions of great gatherings of people at imposing ceremonial complexes for rites dedicated to the renewal of life on earth are surprisingly uniform. First, there is tangible evidence, the scenery and properties of the drama: megaliths; artificial giant mounds or pyramids amounting to artificial mountains; stone and ditch alignments of mathematical sophistication correlating time and space; passage graves and great tholoi, or domed tombs; sacred roads; remains of booths, grandstands, processional ways, and gates—these still survive in awesome combination, with all their cosmic symbolism.

Second is the less tangible evidence of customs, legends, folk festivals, and ancient writings, which together conjure up memories of dramatic and choral celebrations of the creation, culminating in the great creation hymn; ritual contests between life and death, good and evil, and light and darkness, followed by the triumphant coronation of the king to rule for the new age, the progenitor of the race by a sacred marriage; covenants; initiations (including washing and clothing); sacrifices and scapegoats to rid the people of a year of guilt and pollution; and various types of divination and oracular consultation for the new life cycle.

Other Functions of the Temple

Many things surrounding the temple were not essential to its form and function but were the inevitable products of its existence. The words hotel, hospital, and Templar go back to those charitable organizations that took care of sick and weary pilgrims traveling to the holy places. Banking functions arose at the temple since pilgrims brought offerings and needed to exchange their money for animals to be sacrificed, and thus the word money comes from the temple of Juno Moneta, the holy center of the Roman world. Along with that, lively barter and exchange of goods at the great year-rites led to the yearly fair, when all contracts had to be renewed and where merchants, artisans, performers, and mountebanks displayed their wares.

Actors, poets, singers, dancers, and athletes were also part of temple life, the competitive element (the agonal) being essential to the struggle with evil and providing the most popular and exciting aspects of the festivals. The temple’s main drama (the actio) was played by priestly temple actors and royalty. Creation was celebrated with a creation hymn, or poema—the word poem meaning “creation”—sung by a chorus that, as the Greek word shows, formed a circle and danced as they sang.20

The temple was also the center of learning, beginning with the heavenly instructions received there. It was the Museon, or home of the Muses, representing every branch of study: astronomy, mathematics, architecture, and fine arts. People would travel from shrine to shrine exchanging wisdom with the wise, as Abraham did in Egypt. Since the Garden of Eden, or “golden age” motif, was essential to this ritual paradise, temple grounds contained trees and animals, often collected from distant places. Central to the temple school was the library, containing sacred records, including the “Books of Life,” the names of all the living and the dead, as well as liturgical and scientific works.

The temple rites acknowledged the rule of God on earth through his agent and offspring, the king, who represented both the first man and every man as he sat in judgment, making the temple the ultimate seat and sanction of law and government. People met at the holy place for contracts and covenants and to settle disputes.

The Temple and Civilization

All this indicates that the temple is the source, and not a derivative, of the civilizing process. If there is no temple, there is no true Israel; and where there is no true temple, civilization itself is but an empty shell—a material structure of expediency and tradition alone, bereft of the living organism at its center that once gave it life and made it flourish.

Many secular institutions today occupy structures faithfully copied from ancient temples. The temple economy has been perverted along with the rest: feasts of joy and abundance became orgies; sacred rites of marriage were perverted; teachers of wisdom became haughty and self-righteous, demonstrating that anything can be corrupted in this world, and as Aristotle notes, the better the original, the more vicious the corrupted version.

The Restoration and the Temple

Latter-day Saint temples fully embody the uncorrupted functions and meanings of the temple. Did the Prophet Joseph Smith reinvent all this by reassembling the fragments—Jewish, Orthodox, Masonic, Gnostic, Hindu, Egyptian, and so forth? In fact, few of the fragments were available in his day, and those poor fragments do not come together of themselves to make a whole. Latter-day Saints see in the completeness and perfection of Joseph Smith’s teachings regarding the temple a sure indication of divine revelation. This is also seen in the design of the Salt Lake Temple. One can note its three levels; eastward orientation; central location in Zion; brazen sea on the back of twelve oxen holding the waters through which the dead, by proxy, pass to eternal life; rooms appointed for ceremonies rehearsing the creation of the world; and many other symbolic features.

The actual work done within the temple exemplifies the temple idea, with thousands of men and women serving with no ulterior motive. Here time and space come together; barriers vanish between this world and the next, between past, present, and future. Solemn prayers are offered in the name of Jesus Christ to the Almighty. What is bound here is bound beyond, and only here can the gates be opened to release the dead who are awaiting the saving ordinances. Here the whole human family meets in a common enterprise; the records of the race are assembled as far back in time as research has taken them, for a work performed by the present generation to assure that they and their kindred dead shall spend the eternities together in the future. Here, for the first time in many centuries, one may behold a genuine temple, functioning as a temple in the fullest and purest sense of the word.21
